## 第245页

41 House Amulet of John and Georgia
Fig. 41. Plates X-XIV
Phthiotis (Thessaly), Greece IV-V A.D.
Athens, National Museum H. 12.8 em.; W. 3.4 em.
inv. no. 3413 Gold lamella
Lit. JG IX.2,232; Charles Peabody, "A Gnostic Inscription from Athens," Trans
actions and Proceedings of the American Philological Association 28 (1897), pp. xxi
xxiii; J. B. Frey, Corpus lnscriptionum Judaicarum, I (New York, repr. 1952), p. 517,
no. 717; cf. J. Oehler, "Epigraphische Beitrage zur Geschichte des Judentums,"
Monatsschrift for Geschichte und Wissenschaft des Judentums 53 (1909), p. 443, n. l 05;
note A. Audollent, Defixionum Tabellae (Paris, 1904), p. xxxiv, no. (3; K. Preisendanz,
"Die griechischen und lateinischen Zaubertafeln," Archiv for Papyrusforschung 9 (1928),
p. 127.
This amulet, acquired by the Greek Archaeological Society in 1878,
was said to have been found in a grave at an undisclosed site in Phthiotis,
Thessaly. The text requests protection for a house and its inhabitants, John
and Georgia. The long, narrow strip, rounded at the top, was first folded
over from top to bottom a number of times to produce make-shift ruling
lines between which the scribe engraved his text. Some lines thus
produced are uneven and do not appear to run across the width of the
tablet. Furthermore, four or five tiny pin-holes, somewhat evenly spaced,
perforate the middle of the tablet and evidently served to mount the amulet
on a wall somewhere in the house.
The bottom half of the tablet is also inscribed with a thin ruling line
on the top and along the right and left margins, thus delineating the
catalogue of angel-names that predominates this section. A vertical line
through the middle of lines 27-34 further separates the principal list of
angels into two columns- a feature not recognized in the editio princeps.
As a result, the previous readings of these names are largely false, since
the angel-names were read across the width of the tablet, and not in their
separate columns, as intended. A series of names and magic signs near the
bottom of the tablet is also marked off by an etched box.

for Papyrologie und Epigraphik 56 (1984), p. 65, note 11.

we;

## 第250页

41 House Amulet 225
whole, then, is some sort of riddle or proverb. This phrasing seems to
account for the overall sequence of the sentence but makes little sense on a
magic amulet.
3) A third possibility (suggested by K. Maresch) is to read and
restore the letters as follows:
Text: OIIOEANHEIPKELlOO EEHM
Restoration: OIIOEANAE KELlA EEHE
That is, our line preserves a corrupt version of verse, o1rw<; avcteJKeofiavs;.
aarpa7rf!v, " ... in order that you might dissipate the lightning." That then
leaves OtKTJ omp.ovwv, BAfXTTJ<; disconnected.
18-21 OLac/>uA.a~are TOP OLUKOV KB TCx<; 1/;uxi'xc; 'IwaV1JV KB reop')'iav:
Here the plural imperative contrasts, e.g., with the singular Cx7foarpeif;ov,
in line 24, below - a clear indication of originally different sources for
these two 'prayers.' The verb owc/>uA.aaaeLv is common on amulets (see
38,6, Commentary, etc.). But which plural subjects are here addressed?
The plural does not seem to go well with the previous 8A.ar1J<;, which is
singular.
For another house phylactery (also with &ac/>uA.aaaeLv), cf. S.
Eitrem, "A New Christian Amulet," Aegyptus 3 (1922), p. 66f., 2f.: ...
oLac/>vA.a~ov rov olKov rovrov p.eri'x rwv evmKovvrwv a1ro 1ravroc; KaKou,
KTA.; cf. also PGM 2.6: a1raA.A.a~ov rov olKov rovrov, KTA.
The peculiar phonetics of m&Ko<; (i.e. ol{u}Ko<;, read olKo<;), here and
in lines 25 and 49, cannot be explained by normal rules of vowel inter
change: m and u were phonetically equivalent when this tablet was written
(cf. F. T. Gignac, A Grammar of the Greek Papryi I, [Milan, 1976], p.
197f.); however, m and u would never have been written this way
together. The duplication of the two equivalent vowel-sounds side-by-side
can only be explained here as a scribal gloss: a previous exemplar, carry
ing the readings &Kov, vKou, was at some time corrected, interlinearly,
with the diorthOtes writing 'm' directly above the u of vKov: 'm' vKov. Sub
sequently, an unwary copyist re-inserted the 'oL', but without removing
the u. The result is our tablet's owKov (cf. also lines 25, 50). The same
seems to have occurred in line 47, where the spelling an:::teA.ov, is other
wise difficult to explain. Apparently here too a corrector wrote a gamma
above nu, to rectify the spelling -v')'-. Then this gamma got copied into the
word as an extra letter: av'-y'')'eA.A.ov.

## 第251页

226 41 House Amulet
The phrase, "the souls" (rex~ 1/;uxa~). refers to the householders,
John and Georgia, respectively. The noun is best translated "the lives," or
even "people": "Protect the house and its people, John and Georgia."
Note that the personal names that follow are in the accusative (not in the
genitive), yielding the double accusative.
The names Georgia and John are both probably Christian. See, e.g.,
D. Foraboschi, Ono11Ulsticon Alterum Papyrologicum (Milan, 1967), pp.
87, 153, S.VV., rewp"(LCX, rewp"(LO~, 'IwavV1]~. The names are attested
late (not usually earlier than the third century and very common later).
21-22 eeeeewwwww ... xoua17 (.oua17 ed. pr.). Again we have at the
beginning sequences of five vowels; cf. 58,3.
22-23 1JACX.U!!ata '£a{3aw0 'E"AoeeL: 1JACX.L~l!!L~ '£a{3aw0 'E"Ao<e>eL
[v ... ] ed. pr. On La and these other divine names of God, see 7,2; 2,3;
7,2. The sequence 7]Am, comes close to Semitic '7~. Eli, "my God," also
part of the famous utterance of Jesus from the Cross: Matt. 27:46 ( +
var.): 1JAL 1JAL "Aep.a aa{3axOavL I e"AwL e"AwL "Aep.a aa{3axOavL I 1JAL 1JAL
"Aap.a tacf>OavL, etc.
24-25 a1fOCJTpet/;ov 1raJI KCXKOJI a7ro TOV OLJ.IKOU TOVTOU eot, II 001'9J.l.:
[v ... a7ro]rpit/;ov 7ral' KCXKOJI a7ro TOV [otUK]ou TOVTOU ?-Q?- ed. pr. For
a1roarpicf>w used of protecting an owner's land from hail, cf. 11,3 (with
Commentary); 18,24 (?); 53,9. For the general 1rav mKov on amulets, see
57,15 (and Commentary).
26 eot (sign) 7r901'9J.l.: In the middle of line 26, composed of
unrecognizable magic words, is a lambda-shaped 'character' (cf. PGM
VII. 588). The sequence 1r90"9J.l. seems to be repeated in the last line of
this text and may also here represent OV9J.l.l!!.
26-45 Catalogus Angelorum. As noted, this section comprises of
two columns of angel-names demarcated by an inscribed box. The fact
that this was not recognized in the editio princeps lead to the misreading
of all the angel-names in the list. In the Commentary below, the names on
the left side (a) are commented on first, then those on the right (b).
28a 'Apa{3pi1]A: 'Ap"Af3P.LA ed. pr. This is one of the angels of the
sixth encampment in Sepher ha-Razim, p. 39 (according to the pagination
of M. A. Morgan, Sefer Ha-Razim. The Book of Mysteries [Chico,
1983]).

41 House Amulet 227
29a 'Apt~A: Cf. J. Naveh & S. Shaked, Amulets and Magic Bowls
(Jerusalem & Leiden, 1985), A 7:3: 'M',M; cf. J. Michl, "Engel V
(Katalog der Engelnamen)," Reallexikon for Antike und Christentum 5
(1962), p. 204, s. v .. The name is usually interpreted 'Lion of God,' but
see the alphabetical Onomasticum sacrum in PGM 14.2 (Preisendanz, II,
p. 222f.), giving the etymology 'Apt~'A, ¢&x; p.ov Oeov, i.e., as if from
Hebrew ,,M, "light."
29a-30a 'Pa¢a~A: ('f~)/¢a~'A ed. pr. The rho seems to have been
inserted between the letters. The previous editors read the first part of this
name from the end of the preceding line of col. b. On lamellae this angel
occurs in 26,3; 33,3; 38,2; 48,9. Raphael is popular on amulets because
his name means "God heals," or the like. On a silver Aramaic lamella the
name is so etymologically explained: "Raphael, master of healing" ('M~,
M11,CM :J,), in R. Kotansky, "Two Inscribed Jewish Aramaic Amulets
from Syria," Israel Exploration Journal 41 (1991), p. 275, B:3 (with

= C':,·?~; cf. J. Michl, Reallexikon fiir Antike und Christentum 5 (1962),

1] ); letters-forms (zeta, lambda, theta, omicron, etc.; including reversed
lunate epsilon's and kappa's; cf. PGM VII.923, etc.); and more irregular

## 第257页

42 Egyptian Magic Names
Fig. 42. Plate XV
A THENAE (Athens), Greece II A.D.?
Paris, Cabinet des Medailles H. 1.8 em.; W. 2.0 em.
Collection Froehner, no. 285 Gold lamella
Lit. F. Lenormant, in C. Cahier & A. Martin, edd. Melanges d'Archeologie
d'histoire et de litterature, vol. 4 (Paris, 1853), p. 150f.; p. 292; H. Leclercq, art.
"Athenes," Dictionnaire d'Archeologie chretienne et de Liturgie 1/2 (1907), p. 3101; M.
Chabouillet, Catalogue general et raisonne des camees et pierres gravees de Ia
Bibliotheque Imperiale (Paris, 1858), p. 398f., no. 2692; A. Audollent, De.fixionum
o;
Tabellae (Paris, 1904), p. XXXV, no. K. Preisendanz, Archiv for Papyrusforschung 9
(1928), p. 127.
The amulet, said to have been found in Athens, was a donation of
Rochette & Letronne in 1839. It had been folded over in antiquity (twice
from top to bottom) and probably inserted into a suspension capsule. The
text contains only magic syllables, most of which seem to be Egyptian
(kindly identified by H.-J. Thissen). The hand is an upright, round uncial
with no signs of cursive. Each letter is formed separately and disjoined.
The alpha is three-stroked with medial-bar always horizontal (cf. 28). The
hand is probably second century A.D. Later scripts written on gold foil
are always written in a more flowing, cursive hand.

Crete Late Roman or earlier?
London, The British Museum H. 1.0 em.; W. 2.0 em.
Cat. no. 3157 Gold lamella
Lit. F. H. Marshall, Catalogue of the Jewellry Greek, Roman, and Etruscan, in

## 第260页

44 Votive Fragment
Fig. 44
Idaeum Antrum (Mount Ida), Crete Roman Period
Present location unknown H. 2.1 em.; W. 2.1 em.
(no inv. number) Gold lamella
Lit. M. Guarducci, lnscriptiones Creticae (Rome, 1935), vol. 8, no. 8 (p. 97); A.
B. Cook, Zeus.-A Study in Ancient Religion (Cambridge, 1925), vol. 2, part 2, p. 938
('Appendix B' ); F. Halbherr, "Seav i e trovamenti nell' antro di Zeus sui monte Ida in
Creta," Museo Italiano di Antichitil Classica 2 (1888), pp. 689-768, esp. p. 751 (with P.
Orsi, idem, pp. 769-904).
This fragment of gold foil represents one of many votive offerings
discovered at the cave-sanctuary of Mount Ida, near Gortyn (Cook
1925:938), including a number of thin gold discs decorated with dots or
rays; a gold decorative plaque of the 'sub-Minoan' era; and "an oblong
plate of gold to which three draped female figures, with bucrania between
them, are soldered; from the plate hangs a snake biting its tail" (Cook
1925:935; Halbherr 1888:752). The cave for centuries had served as a
depository of votive gifts, and our amulet may have been deposited as a
thank-offering by someone healed from a medical complaint.

## 第261页

45 Christian Amulet for Health and Favor
Fig. 45. Plate XV
Laodicea ad mare (Latakia), Syria V-VI A.D.
Jerusalem H. 5.0 em.; W. 3.0 em.
(Private Collection) Gold lamella
Lit. L. Jalabert & R. Mouterde, Inscriptions grecques et latines de la Syrie, vol.
4: Laodicee. Apamene (Paris, 1955), p. 38f., no. 1284.
Mouterde' s editio princeps of this piece, first published in 1955,
gave a largely unreliable reading (see below). In 1990 the piece resurfaced
in the antiquities market at which time I re-examined it from autopsy. The
gold tablet is particularly thick, and hence the scribe found difficulty in
engraving the letters, which are often quite irregularly shaped. The hand
is probably to be assigned to the 5th or 6th century A.D.

+ For health(?) (magic signs) Eloai. By the vigorous name <and> crown

1 + Ilpoc; C!JI-7!Lav: The letters are badly transcribed. Mouterde sug
gested the reading Ilpoc; CxTJTicxv ( = cx~Ticxv), "Pour une accusation."
Given the occurrence twice, below, of iryicxv, and the clumsily written
letter-forms it is probable that Ilpoc; YV!1'Y.ucxv preserves a miscopying of
an original 1rpoc; iryicxv (= iryeicxv), "for health" -a reading that at least
follows established precedence. The incipit-formula 1rpoc; + (the name of
an affliction or desired wish) is common on amulets. No other reading
(e.g., IIpoc; !'Y1f:WV, Ilpoc; f't).JI-1f:Lcxv) seems to yield any sense appropriate
to an amulet. A person's name would not be expected here: a reading such
as Ilpoc; · AJL7r < e/1. > uxv, "For Ampelia," for example, though not
impossible would have to assume a novel use of the 1rpoc;-incipit.
2-3 Magic signs: For such common 'ringlet'-type letters, cf. PGM
XXXVI.263; XLIX; LX; Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. II, pl. X, 92,
etc. On the fourth 'character,' note esp. PGM VII.390-395; VII.420f.

articulated 'E}\wcxi, e < cxL); see 2, lf.; 38,1; 41,23, etc. In the editio
princeps, the letters in line 4 were wrongly resolved as an abbreviation,
ileA( Tiov) - see below on lines 5-7. Line 5 was altogether omitted both
in the drawing and in the transcription.

evTovo(v), ("talisman regulier, efficace"), ed. pr. In the reading
< o> voJLcx it is possible to interpret one of the 'eyelets' of the neighbor
ing magic sign as an omicron, hence ovoJLcx. For Ka = KcxTa, note F. T.
Gignac, Grammar of the Greek Papyri I (Milan, 1976), p. 313. Since the
writing within the tabula ansata gives a separate sentence, the To at the
end of line 6 goes with the following adjective evTovo(v), the first part of
which is written vertically, outside the left edge of the tabula ansata. The
"strong" or "vigorous" name must refer to the preceding name of God,
Eloai.
In the editio princeps this To was read with the remainder of the text
as follows: om[v] V1r7JVcX < xOTJ > v < e > ~c; XAOLV, 'ETi¢cxvov KV(piov)

OLOovc; V,eiav, awT1Jpiav, KTA.
Tabula ansata (A-C): There are several possible readings of these
lines. Clearly the reading of the initial letters TriAN, though clumsily
written (the --yL- is written close together like a 1r), gives either the accusa
tive v-yiav, or better, the future, V)'LO!VW, "I shall be healthy." Perhaps the
best possible reading is an m, rather than w, at the end: v-yimvcxt (read, m
= e, v-yiawe). The verb v-ytaivetv occurs in PGM VII.255 (Kvpte,
v-yimve, "Avxve); XXIIb 28 (v-yimve "Avxve); PGM 9.13,30 (o1rwc; v-yuxvw;
rva Te"Aeiwc; v-ytavw).
The rest of the text can be transcribed as follows: 00<; Ttl' e(v)XO!PLV,
"give favor" (rather than, e.g., ooc; Ttl' {e} xapw, or ooc; 7rve). On the
feminine noun, evxaptc; ("grace, charm"), see Lampe, A Patristic Greek
Lexicon, s.v. F. T. Gignac, Grammar of the Greek Papyri, I (Milan,
1976), pp. 228f. gives numerous examples of ev > e in the papyri.

(Magic signs). Release Juliana from all sorcery and from all passive suffering
and all active influence and demonic apparition of the night and day; now,
now; quickly, quickly; immediately, immediately, immediately.
Giron 1922:66 states that the amulet comes from the area of Aleppo.
The blank tablet was probably first folded, then inscribed, and re-folded
again, judging from the fact that the written text appears to fall con
sistently between the creases in foil (see introduction to 32).
Jalabert & Mouterde's dating of the letter-forms to the 4th or 5th
century is too late; the script is a good, 2nd-century, semicursive, literary
hand. A conservative attribution would assign the text to the late 2nd or
possibly early 3rd century A.D. The overall style- notably the smallish
omicron's (and similar letters), the alpha's sigma's, and mu's - is com
parable to the hand of P. Oxy. VI. 853 (late II A.D.).
1-8 Magic signs: The second 'character' in line 1 compares well
with those of no. 1, lf.; PGM 1.268; and VII. 922; the first 'character,' of
line 3 is similar to that found in PGM II .155.
8-12 "AuCJare r~v 'Iov"ALav~v a1ro 1raCJ17<;{ 11d ¢apf.1-aKiac;: Juliana
seeks to be freed of the influence of sorcery or poisoning, as
¢apf.1-aK(e)iac; can mean both. The plural imperative addresses the names
and 'characters' listed in the first part of the spell; "AuCJaTe, "loosen,"
further suggests that Juliana believes herself already bound by the
influence of some ¢apf.1-aKia; see also 36,15f.; 52,12,76,96f. Our spell is
thus a 'counter-charm,' perhaps sexual in orientation (see Commentary
below). For the combination of expressions similar to those found here,
see PGM XIII.253: 1rpoc; "Aumv ¢apf.1-aKwv; PGM XXXVI. 259: "AuCJare
1r&v ¢apf.1-aKov; cf. also PGM IV. 2176: ¢apf.1-am vLK~CJeLc;; A. Delatte,
Anecdota Atheniensia I (Paris & Liege, 1927), p. 245,23f.: opKi!;w CJe,

and ivip-yeLa must represent opposite pairs of magic operations (c f. the

wt ~p.ipac;). Since the genitive vuKroc; Kat ~p.ipac; is, for all purposes,

our counter-charm envisions real threats of a sexual nature designed to
work against its holder, Julia. This is clearly the tone of another
pharmakon-oriented spell, no. 52, a spell that, though mentioning general
protection from 'compelling daimons, sorceries, and binding spells' (lines
8-13), also specifies less ambiguous sexual motives behind the real writing
of the tablet (lines 95-101). The fact that this Aleppo spell is written for a
woman seeking a counter-measure against 1r6.0oc; and evep-yeia insinuates
that the 'sorcery' and threat of 'demonic visitation' may be sexual in
nature. The demonic appearances 'of the night or day' recalls, then, the
actual nekydaimon sent on behalf of the male aggressor to assault his
sexual target. The Latin amulet (no. 24) gives a good example of such a
demon being sent to a woman for obstensibly sexual aims.

Fig. 47. Plate XVI
EPIPHANIA (Ramah), Syria IV-V A.D.
Copenhagen, National Museum H. 2.4 em.; W. 3.0 em
8A 256 N7 Bat. V. room K (10) 13.5 1938 Gold lamella
Lit. Adam Biilow-Jacobsen, in G. Ploug, ed. Hama. Fouilles et recherches, 1931-
1938: ll/.1. The Graeco-Roman Town (Copenhagen, 1985), p. 250, App. 2 (fig. 64).
This gold tablet was found in the room of a building (Bat. V, room
K) in 1938 among the extensive urban excavations of the Roman town of
Hama. Biilow-Jacobsen (1985:250) describes the piece as follows:
When found it was folded once vertically and rolled up tightly. The right
half, which has suffered most, seems to have been innermost in the roll. The
left, right and bottom margins are marked with a thin line scratched about 1
mrn from the edge. An inscription of seven lines of cursive Greek letters of
the fourth or fifth century of our era is scratched and pressed into the surface
with a dry point made of material a little harder than the gold, presumably of
bronze.
The inscription is extremely difficult to read because of its small size, its cur
sive character and the damage it has suffered when rolled and unrolled.
It seems unlikely that much of the editio princeps can be improved upon,
though several suggestions are made below.

## 第273页

48 An Amulet from Emesa
Fig. 48. Plate XVII
EMESA (Horns), Syria Early I B.C.
Leiden, Museum of Antiquities H. 7.0 em.; W. 6.0 em.
Inv. no. B 1952/7.1 Silver lamella
Lit. B. H. Stricker, "De grote zeeslang," Mededelingen en Verhanddelingen No.
10 van het Vooraziatisch-Egyptisch Genootschap "Ex Oriente Lux" (Leiden, 1953), Figs.
6 & 7; P. J. Sijpesteijn, "A Syrian Phylactery on a Silver Plate," Oudheidkundige
Mededelingen uit het Rijksmuseum van Oudheden te Leiden 59-60 (1978-1979), pp.189-
192; pl. 37.
This piece, acquired in 1952, is said to have come from Horns,
Syria. Sijpesteijn 1978-1979 provided a welcome re-edition of Stricker's
editio princeps which appeared in Dutch with no commentary.
The hand, which has not previously been assigned a date, looks
quite early, to judge from comparable Egyptian papyri. D. Hagedorn
privately confirms the impression that the script's appearance retains many
features distinctive of a late Ptolemaic script. The broad, tapering letters
-often joined by an elongated connector or 'dash' -are markedly pre
Roman in look. Alpha's, lambda's, xi's, nu's and mu's are all quite wide,
being broadly written with a splayed, flattened-out appearance, with their
right diagonals sloping to a near-horizontal position. The whole lends the
script the 'clothesline' appearance characteristic of Ptolemaic hands (note,
in particular, the Ou&iJA. To of line 11). The rho's and phi's also carry
rather long 'tails' that angle leftwards at the tip. The epsilon's with its
detached curved upper-stroke is also due to Ptolemaic influence, as are the
nu's with high-sweeping right verticals. Note also the peculiar zeta begun
with a long, initial downstroke (observed by R. Daniel). Furthermore, the
manner of writing the combination of e + "• with the epsilon's middle
stroke and the left vertical of the nu formed by a single motion of the
stylus, is also a tell-tale indicator of a late Ptolemaic hand. Note also the ex
+ p, and such similar letter combinations; cf. P. Mert. I, 6 (77 B.C.). P.
Wash. 11.74 (II-I B.C. = "Description of Magical Figures") also offers
parallels in some respects.

## 第274页

48 An Amulet from Emesa 249
Particularly noteworthy is the extended connecting-stroke following
the eta (e.g., in the angel-names ending -rJA). In Egypt, such a feature
does not normally survive the Augustan age, being practically extinct by
the second half of the 1st century A.D. In areas outside of Egypt (e.g., in
Syria), such a stroke may have survived somewhat longer (see
palaeographical discussion on 36, 39, etc.; cf. W. Schubart, Griechische
Palaeographie [Munich, 1925], p.124, Abb. 120). But even though the
city of our amulet's provenance, Emesa, enjoyed considerable autonomy
from both Seleucid and Roman rule, there is nothing to suggest that the
script of this amulet is a late survivor of some conservative scribal habit.
Emesa had its own dynastic monarchs at least as early as the mid-second
century B.C. - rulers whose names became known only in the first
century B.C. (viz., Iamblichos, Samsigeramos, Sohaemus, etc. See,
Richard D. Sullivan, "The Dynasty of Emesa," Aufstief und Niedergang
der romischen Welt 11.8 [1977], pp. 198-217; further, J. D. Grainger, The
Cities of Seleukid Syria [Oxford, 1990], pp. 178-181).

singular active). It is also possible to interpret the name as 1~i~, somer,

## 第278页

48 An Amulet from Emesa 253
"guard," "watcher," etc., with the imperative, t'a, affixed (='Come,' as
in the famous Christian acclamation MapavaOa). In either case, it derives
from the same Semitic word and is appropriate to amulets of this kind,
being the equivalent of the usual Greek imperative, <t>v"Aa~ov, as noted in
Phillip, loc. cit., p. 53 (no. 51).
7-8 'A(3amp(3a: The name may be an imperfect palindrome:
a(3 <p > amp(3a, according to Sijpesteijn, who cites a(3aKeara in A.
Delatte & Ph. Derchain, Les intailles magiques greco-egyptiennes (Paris,
1964), no. 476. Very close to our sequence here is CJOVJJ.apm + amp(3a
in PGM O(stracon) 2.21. It may be no coincidence that aKap(3a anwers to
Aramaic 1'91i?Y. caq(a)raba', "scorpion." That would make our Abakarba
a deity whose name means "Father ( = 'Progenitor,' 'Chief') of Scor
pions." Putatively, as the 'Ancestor of Scorpions,' Abarkaba would be the
god who controlled scorpions. On 'scorpion' -spells in general, see P.
Wash. Univ. II (edd. K. Maresch & Zola M. Packman). The name may
also be a version or corruption of a(3pamf>ap(3a, the ABRACADABRA of
medieval and modern magic first attested in the 2nd cent. A.D. in Q.
Serenus Sammonicus Liber Medicinalis, LI.935 (ed. F. Vollmer, Corpus
Medicorum Latinorum, II.3 [1916]), p. 45: inscribes chartae quod dicitur
abracadabra saepius et subter repetes, etc. and found also in Greek letters
on a gemstone in Mouterde, Glaive de Dardanos, p. 15 (cited above).
8 Beevxwxwx B~wxwvtwx: Variants of Bawxwwwwx, the widely
discussed 'Spirit of Darkness' (Egyptian B3-n-kkw), for which see W.
Brashear, "Glossary," in Aufstieg und Niedergang der Romischen Welt
(forthcoming). The name, as usually spelled, has an isopsephic value of
3663 (a palindromic number). The spellings found here yield 3462 and
2923, respectively -numbers of no apparent significance. See on the name
and its isopsephic value, Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. I, no. 15,3f.,
with Commentary.

PVTG 3; Leiden, [1970], p. 32):

apxayye'AwP. Ms. G2].
In the list above, only Eapt~A differs slightly from the tablet's
Eovpt~A (the Ethiopic reads Saraqael), and a seventh angel, 'Pep.et~A,
(attested only in G2) is different from our Ovot~A (all other Mss., includ
ing the Ethiopic, have only the six angels). The interchange between the
two similar-sounding angel-names, Eovpt~A and Eapt~A (and Saraqael), is
not unexpected. Nor does the slight variation in manuscript-traditions
giving six versus seven angels diminish the significance of our parallel
lists: because all Greek manuscripts state that there should be seven angels
listed - though six are usually preserved - our Ov&L~}-.. must then
represent an important independent witness and variant to G2's 'Pep.et~A.
The fact that the very names in the two lists - and their exact
sequence- match so closely makes the likelihood of a reliance on a com
mon source highly probable. The presence of the rare 'Pa-yov~}-.. shows all
the more that the similarity between the angel-list of I Enoch and that of
the Emesa tablet cannot be accidental. Was the composer of the Emesa
amulet familiar with Enoch or its underlying traditions? Given I Enoch's
complex angelology and its interest in magic (e.g., ch. VIII, 3f.:
Eep.taS"'cu; ioioa~ev i-Traotoa~ m"i intorop.ia~, etc.), its seems likely that
early 'Enochian' literature of some kind would have been consulted by
serious compilers of magic charms. The text of our amulet, though not
Enochian, per se, does provide an early witness to some angelological
fragment that the two documents must have shared in common.
The chapter in which the catalogue of angels appears belongs to the
so-called Book of the Watchers (I Enoch 1-36), a document commonly
judged to be one of the oldest sections of I Enoch (dated to the 3rd
century B.C.); see George W. E. Nickelsburg, art. "Enoch, First Book
of," in D. N. Freedman, ed. Anchor Bible Dictionary (1992), pp. 509,

## 第280页

48 An Amulet from Emesa 255
516, with all the standard secondary literature- dated later by E. Isaac,
in J. H. Charlesworth, ed. The OT Pseudepigrapha, I (New York, 1983),
p. 6f. The original composition of our Emesan amulet in Greek must be
somewhat later than the recent Aramaic fragments of I Enoch found at
Qumran, but roughly contemporary with the Greek versions thought to
have been written in the period ca. 1st cent. B.C.- 1st cent. A.D.
Ragouel would be Aramaic raea 'el, "will of God" (from l-t1Y,1,
racwa'). Ovbt~A is perhaps from Tlitot 'OJ, "omen, sign;" or, from 1iY, cod,
"existence, strength,"(= 'El exists/endures'); cf. also i11~, cedah
("assembly; gathering; band," - an appropriate name for angelic hosts).
Souriel is probably ?tot',,l ("God is my Rock"). The other names and
their meanings are widely known and discussed at 26, etc.; cf. R.
Kotansky, Israel Exploration Joumal41 (1991), p. 278.
11-12 To fw-ypa¢r1f.J.a T[ov] Oeov: On fw-ypa¢ruw, Sijpesteijn (1978-
79:191) observes: 1) fw-ypa¢rJIJ.a hitherto occurs in Plato, Philebus 39d
and Cratylus 430bff. and Plutarch, Moralia 410a; 2) it has the same
meaning as the more usual word fcj>owv; and 3) the words TO fw-ypa¢ruw
Toil Oeov, "probably meant that on this plate the picture of a certain god
had to be engraved but that these words were simply taken over without
any real understanding," and "that the magical handbooks served as
examples from which formulae for actual use were copied." On this
phenomenon in general, note (in addition to the examples in Sijpestein, ad
loc. ), F. Maltomini, "Appunti magici," Zeitschrift for Papyrologie und
Epigraphik 66 (1986), p. 159. Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. I, no.
38,13 contains a similar reference from a formulary: eanv be Ta fi{Jbw
TavTa, "these are the figures" (with the commentary: " ... mistakenly
copied from a formulary," p. 122).
For references to 'pictures of God,' as here, note Philo, Decal. 70,
where idolators are said to believe that the images and pictures they make
represent the gods they depict: Ta o' inr' BK8ti'WI' O'YJJ.l.LOVP"f'YJOivra

49 Magic Names or Cipher
Fig. 49
EMESA (Horns), Syria IV-V A.D.
Present location unknown H.l4.1 em.; W. 3.3 em.
(IGLS V.2494) Silver lamella
Lit. L. Jalabert, R. Mouterde, C. Mondesert, edd. Inscriptions grecques et latines
de la Syrie, Tome V: Emesene (lnstitut Fran~aise d'archeologie de Beyrouth. Bibliotheque
archeologique et historique LXVI; Paris, 1959), p. 208; no. 2494.
This silver tablet was first communicated by H. Seyrig in 1935 and
apparently formed part of the Collection Mouterde. Its present location is
not known, though a number of magic gemstones and amulets formerly
belonging to and published by Mouterde have recently resurfaced in some
European antiquities markets. Among these I have also examined and re
read the silver lamella formerly described in the same Inscriptions grec
ques et latines de Ia Syrie ( = IGLS) catalogue as that described here
( = 49). That text (IGLSyr V, no. 2495) is described as follows: "Lamelle
d'argent, h. de 0 m. 07. Au debut, 7 li. de signes et de lettres frustes. Au
dessous 12 li. d'un texte semitique ou /'on reconnalt deux l. grecques." It
should be herewith reported that this piece is indeed written in Semitic
(with only scattered Greek letters or magic signs EEE, ZHO, eeET).
The text of 49 is puzzling as it contains no meaningful Greek, and
the voces magicae are not the usual sort of angel-names. They are largely
unattested. The tablet is divided into a number of separate sections by
ruling lines or boxes. The uppermost of these contains a list of names,
most of which are made up of seven letters each (lines 1, 2, 3, 4, 7, 8,
10, 13). Each also seems to begin with a seemingly good Greek syllable:
cry-, Kw</Ja-, {3of...-, va- (?), O(e)t-y-, TaJL-, Ct<JTp-, ACXJL-, (JKW-, 'YfVKT- (?),
oKra-, aJL<P-. If so, we are perhaps dealing with a cipher, cryptogram, or
a system of scrambled words. A not dissimilar list of pseudo-Greek, given
in columnar form, is also preserved in Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag
II, no. 96 A, 24-43: ~JLW<bv~, ~JLWXV~, in5w7rvi, avOpW"f8V~, avopwp,
~JLW"f8V~, KOpKw{3av"i'Yj, XPV<JW{3ef...f...~, XPV<JWKWJLB, KPOJLVAeVKOV,

## 第283页

258 49 Magic Names or Cipher
p.evovA.aO, xap(3a0a, aOwp.(3av"A.rJ, tav~p.va, xwvovOa, p.evov(3a, (3e"Aep0l,
taxOarJp, xa"Awv{3rJ. It is more likely, however, that the names are Egyp
tain or Semitic (cf. Ea(3aw0, in line 20; and the Commentary on
aarovprop., line 17, (3a"Ap.w0w, line 21, etc.).
The first editors assign the tablet to the 4th or 5th century A. D., a
dating we cannot quarrel with, given there is no published photograph.

mea, "belly; womb" (a fertility goddess?). The second ,, n e a , m e, oup1rop,
would seem to represent Hebrew 1iM, 'or, "light," + pur, "lot,"
"fate," ( = Babylonian puru, "lot") - the latter especially in reference to
the Festival of Purim, in the Book of Esther. The origins of the festival
are unknown, though most scholars say it is pagan and is originally
associated with Babylonian Isthar, whose name has been also identified in
'Esther' and, coincidentally, in line 7 and in the next word on this tablet.
On Purim, Esther, and Ishtar, see the discussion in C. A. Moore, art.
"Esther, Book of," in D. N. Freedman, The Anchor Bible Dictionary, II
(1992) p. 637, citing P. Jensen, "Elamitische Eigennamen: Ein Beitrag
zur ErkUirung der elamitischen Inscriften," Wiener Zeitschrift for die
Kunde des Morgenlandes 6 (1892), pp. 47-70; 209-226.
17 aarouproJl.: The first part of this word recalls Ishtar, the major
Mesopotamian deity corresponding to Venus (cf. 'or, "light," above). The
Aramaic word for Venus, ,;,noM, 'isthar, is the equivalent of Ishtar
(m·n~Y). The West-Semitic counterpart, 'Astarte' (= 'Asthoreth') occurs
in Biblical Hebrew. The syllable TOJl., then, must be related to C~T-1, "to be
complete, blameless" - here, probably the participle ( = 'Ishtar is blame
less'); cf. also CJ;I (tam) "pure," blameless" (an epithet), and c·n (tom)
"perfection; completeness."
19 TOJ1.W{3'YJ: A magic name whose meaning could also be explained
as containing Semitic C~rl (tmm): ':lM ,~·n, tom'i 'abi, "My Father is per
fect," or "My perfection (is in) my Father" (that is, 'My integrity is in my
God,' as the construction can thus connote); cf. TCXJJ.'YJ-, line 6, above.
20 Ea{3aw0: 'Lord of Hosts.' One of the few known magical names
on this tablet; cf. 2,2f.; 7,2; 12,14f. ('Index V,' s.v.).
21 {3a"AJ1.tv0w: The original editors locate the name in Egyptian
magic (balmenthre, unexplained), in F. Ll. Griffith & F. Thompson,

Demotic Magical Papyrus, p. 139 (vol. 3). = PDM x1v. 465ff., J.

## 第287页

50 Magic Text
Fig. 50
HELIOPOLIS (Ba'albek), Lebanon IV-Y A.D.
Musee du Beyrouth H. 4.5 em.; W. 8.0 em.
(no inv. number) Gold lamella
Lit. Rene Mouterde, "Amulette en Or," in: Maurice Chehab, Mosai"que du Liban.
Text: Bulletin du Musee de Beyrouth 14 (1958), p. Slf.; fig. 2; pl. 26.
Mouterde read and published this piece in 1958, shortly after it was
unearthed in a sepulcher near the ancient city of Baalbek. The piece comes
from the Villa de Soueidiye, situated between Baalbeck and Ras el-' Ain,
which the Service des Antiquites du Liban excavated. Soueidiye, a villa
and residential district, is known primarily for its mosaics uncovered there
at the Villa and Triclinium.
Mouterde provided a text with facsimile; however, the two
photographs, labelled 'Avers' and 'Revers,' were in fact both printed in
'mirror-image.' Furthermore, his drawing and text often do not seem to
correspond to the letters visible on the photograph. Without a good
photograph for study, little can be done to improve the text (a request for
a photograph in 1979, and again in 1993, was denied). Nonetheless, the
text should be included in this corpus as an example of a published piece
with known Fundort. Hopefully a new edition of this piece can be
properly provided someday; a few suggested readings have been made
from examining the published photographs held up to a light.
Mouterde's Reading:

## 第290页

51 A Jewish 'Exorcism' of the Womb
Fig. 51
BERYTUS (Beirut), Lebanon I B.C.-I A.D.
Paris, Cabinet des Medailles H. 2.8 em.; W. 2.3 em.
Collection Froehner, no. 287 Gold lamella
Lit. F. Lenormant, in: C. Cahier & A. Martin, edd. Melanges d'Archeologie
d'histoire et de litterature, IV (Paris, 1853), pp. 152-156; CJG IV. 9062 (Curtius and
Kirchoff); H. Leclercq, art. "Amulettes," Dictionnaire d'Archeologie chretienne et de
Liturgie Ill (1907), col. 1796 [hereafter, DACL, below] (w/ the readings of Lenormant &
Kirchoff); idem, art. "Exorcisme, Exorciste," DACL V/1 (1922), col. 968; idem, art.
"Extreme-Onction," DACL V/1 (1922), col. 1031; F. Cabrol, "Acclamations," DACL,
Ill (1907), cols. 258f. (facs.); F. X. Kraus, "Amulets," Real-Encyklopiidie der Christ
lichen Alterthamer l (1880), p. 51; M. Chabouillet, Catalogue general et raisonne des
camees et pierres gravees de la Bibliotheque Imperiale (Paris, 1858), p. 399f., no. 2693;
K. Wessely, Wiener Studien 8 (1889), p. l78f.; note K. Preisendanz, Archiv jar Papyrus
forschung 9 (1928), p. 132; Louis Perret, Catacombes de Rome, vol. 4 (Paris, 1851), pl.
16, no. 76 & 77 (capsule).
The tiny gold lamella was acquired in 1852 by the Cabinet des
Medailles, Paris. It was apparently found in a gold capsule near Beirut,
Syria and was bequeathed by Froehner (cat. no. 287). Lenormant first
published it with a reading somewhat different from that of CIG and
attributed great importance to the text as a witness of the Christian prac
tice of extreme unction. Hence the amulet is still referred to as Christian
in all subsequent publications. A re-examination from autopsy, however,
shows that the piece has been misread and is not Christian at all - in fact
it may even be pre-Christian in date. The text is rather a phylactery to
stop a woman's uterus from roaming throughout the body - a popular
magico-medical belief thought to be responsible for a host of medical
complaints and psychic disorders. Note the description of the phenomenon
in A. A. Barb & J. Gwyn Griffiths, Journal of the Warburg and
Courtauld Institut 22 (1959), p. 370: "This, of course, is not the womb in
its blessed, child-bearing aspect, but the roaming, evil Hystera, which
according to ancient belief was thought responsible for all kinds of pain
affecting bowels, stomach, heart, chest, and even suffocation." The whole

## 第291页

266 51 'Exorcism' of the Womb
matter has been recently discussed in Jean-Jacques Aubert, "Threatened
Wombs: Aspects of Ancient Uterine Magic," Greek, Roman, and
Byzantine Studies 30 (1989), pp. 421-449, a detailed study that now sup
plements A. A. Barb's classic essay, "Diva Matrix," Journal of the War
burg and Courtauld Institute 16 (1953), pp. 193-238; see also R. K. Rit
ner, "A Uterine Amulet in the Oriental Institute Collection," Journal of
Near Eastern Studies 43 (1984), pp. 209-221.
The hand is to be assigned an early date, especially in view of the
prevalence of the connecting 'dash' following eta - a feature common in
the 1st century B.C., but becoming increasingly scarce by the 1st century
A.D. Our scribe still uses this stroke between nu-alpha in line 4 and
around either end of the omega of line 11. Tau is formed with a single
down-stroke followed by a separated horizontal dash. Kappa is of the
looped variety.

A similar spell, to be written on a tin lamella, is preserved in PGM

favorably with the general language of our text: IIpo~ f.J.~Tpa~ aval>pof.J.~"·
i~opKitw ae, f.J.~Tpav, KTA., "For the 'running up' of the womb: 'I adjure

the uterus, see also Aelius Promotus, 'IarptKa <PvatKa Kat avn7ra0TJnKa,

1 'E~opKitw ae: Lit. "I am placing you under oath" (to do some
thing). The formula is widely found, though it is relatively uncommon on
amulets. When the formula does occur on amulets, it is usually of Jewish
origin, as here. The Aramaic equivalent is Y:lili~, "to adjure," "impose an
oath" (the Af form of Y:liZi, "to swear"). For its use on Aramaic amulets,
cf. J. Naveh & S. Shaked, Amulets and Magic Bowls (Jerusalem &
Leiden, 1985), A 12:(9); A 8:3; 9:4; A 1:21, etc.; cf. 52,1 (Com
mentary). See, further, H. Leclercq, art. "Adjuration," Dictionnaire
d'Archeologie chretienne et de Liturgie IIi (1907), pp. 527-535. Here the
adjuration formula, properly speaking, belongs to the phrase i1ri rii>
ovof.J.an ... f.J.BPetv, "in name of the . . . remain at your place."
2-3 f.J.~Tpa 'Jtj;a~ I ijv ereKev '11/;a: (;, Eamvv&~ KfXL ar(avpe') f.J.B
vitj;ov Lenormant: JL[ ou?] Cx7raA.A.aaiTJP' [ ev ]f,J.ePi[t]o Curtius-Kirchoff.
These lines, as read by previous editors, gave rise to the long-standing
view that this text was an early Christian amulet dealing with extreme
unction. This interpretation can now be abandoned; what we have in these
line is nothing other than the usual matrilinear formula, on which see D.
R. Jordan, "CIL VIII 19525 (B).2 QPVVLVA = q(uem) p(eperit) vulva,"
Philologus 120 (1976), pp. 127-132.
What is odd is that a form of Ipsa is used for both daughter and
mother. Ipsa as a personal name is unexpected and not previously attested;
hence, the reading probably disguises some kind of cipher used for the
names of both. It is likely that Greek '11/;a represents nothing other than a
transcription of Latin ipsa, "herself," the equivalent of the usual ilia used
in such formulations as illi us, quem peperit ilia; cf., e. g., Marcellus

## 第293页

268 51 'Exorcism' of the Womb
Empiricus De medic. 36.70: Carmen idioticum, quod lenire podagram
dicitur, sic ... : 'fuge fuge podagra et omnis nervorum dolor de pedibus
meis et omnibus membris meis,' aut si alii praecantas, dices 'illius, quem
peperit ilia.' See also 7,6; 8,5. In 40,12 a version of the same seems to
occur in its Greek equivalent: ... iJ.CiAH1TCX oe 7rpC)I; 01' Oet\L avrn, but
II •••
particularly with him whom (she) herself wishes." Although the presence
of Latin in Greek letters in the Beirut spell might seem unusual at first
glance, such a combination of several languages within a few lines of a
short spell is relatively common with such amulets (cf. 2,7,8,18, etc.).
Ancient Berytus was already made a Roman colony by 14 B.C., so the
presence of Latin forms in a Greek text at this time would not be surpris
ing. It simply attests to what we already know of the language milieu of
the whole region at the dawn of the Christian era.
The use of ipsa also shows that the text of this amulet was originally
copied from a formulary. The amulet was never 'personalized' by the
insertion of the bearer's real name and that of the mother.

## 第295页

52 The Great Angelic Hierarchy
Fig. 52a-c. Plate XVIII
BERYTUS (Beirut), Lebanon IV A.D.
Paris, Musee du Louvre H. 37.5 em.; W. 3.0 em.
Bj 88 (Inv. M.N.D. 274) Silver lamella
Lit. A. Heron de Villefosse, "Tablette magique de Beyrouth conservee au Musee
du Louvre," Florilegium ou Recueil de travaux d'erudition dedies a Monsieur LeMar
quis Melchior de Vogile (Paris, 1909), pp. 287-295; D. R. Jordan, "A New Reading of a
Phylactery from Beirut," Zeitschrift filr Papyrologie und Epigraphik 88 (1991 ), pp. 61-
69; H. Leclercq, art. "Enchantement," Dictionnaire d'Archeologie chretienne et de
Liturgie VIi (1922), pp. 33-36; Villefosse-Michon, Musee du Louvre, IV. Department
des antiquites grecques et romains, acquisitions 1900, n. 76; note also PGM XXXV,
app. crit. ad loc.; L. Robert, "Amulettes grecques," Journal des Savants, 1981, p. 10f.;
J. G. Gager, Curse Tablets and Binding Spells from the Ancient World (New York &
Oxford, 1992), no. 125, pp. 232-233.
This unusually long piece, written across the width of a narrow strip
of silver, was found in bronze tubular capsule in a tomb at Beirut. The
editio princeps of de Villefosse is now superseded by Jordan (1991 :61-
69), who publishes photographs for the first time along with a greatly
improved reading. The text given below follows his transcription, with
minor changes noted.
The perfunctory purpose of the spell is to protect a certain
Alexandra from demonic afflictions and a host of magical procedures
thought to be of potential threat during the course of her mundane, day-to
day activities. Some of these procedures threaten to malign her sexually
-or at least she is felt to be so threatened (lines 78f.; 98; 101f.). But it
is not clear how 'personalized' the charm really is in these sections, as the
text there seems to follow patterned formulas.
More importantly, before the spell actually addresses the specific
complaints plaguing Alexandra, a long litany of angelic names is given,
identifying the heaven in which each rules and the realm of influence over
which each executes authority. This type of catalogue shows all the
earmarks of a well-circulated and widely documented tractate that
seemingly had an independent existence long before it found particular

## 第296页

52 An Angelic Hierarchy 271
expression as a part of the Beirut spell. Traces and vestiges of similar
angelic catalogues - sometimes containing verbatim citations and other
comparative materials - are found scattered in various literatures, some
magical, others of a more pseudepigraphic and apocryphal nature. The
distinction is largely immaterial, as all indications point back to some
original document - or body of documents - probably deriving from the
Hellenistic Jewish community of Alexandria, a document sectarian in
nature, concerned with a specific angelic teaching, probably exorcistic in
application, and perpetrated in the scribal transmission of some esoteric
synagogal liturgies. Possible connections with Merkabah speculation,
Enochian literature, the Hekaloth, Qumran, Solomonic lore, or Jewish
Gnosticism, though reasonably conceivable, cannot be addressed here.
Examples of magic texts that seem to be based either more or less
directly on a Vorlage shared in common with that of the Beirut silver
lamella, or that otherwise show a very close affinity to it, include the fol
lowing:
1) An unpublished gold lamella in Bern (see Jordan 1991:61).
2) An unpublished silver phylactery in the Fitzwilliam Museum.
3) PGM XXXV (=PSI I. 29).
4) A carnelian gemstone: A. Delatte & Ph. Derchain, Les intailles
magiques greco-egyptiennes (Paris, 1964), p. 316, no. 460; L. Robert,
Journal des Savants, 1981, pp. 7f. (a text that apparently parallels only a
small portion of the Bern gold lamella).
5) PGM XXIIb.1-26, the so-called IIpoaevx~ 'la.Kw{j, a text that
may contain a related archangelic hierarchy, but the condition of which is
so fragmentary as to discourage any substantive comparison.
6) Furthermore, a lead defixio from Hadrumetum ( = A. Audollent,
Defixionum Tabellae, Paris, 1904, no. 271; with excellent commentary by
R. Wunsch in Antike Fluchtafeln [Bonn, 1912 = Kleine Texte], pp. 21-26;
Nr. 5) contains certain 'liturgical' parallels to some portions of the above
mentioned texts. Although preserving no portion of the strictly angelologi
cal hierarchy itself, relevant lines invite comparison with the text of the
Beirut amulet and are taken up below, where applicable. Further, a related
text can also be found in a group of Carthaginian lead tablets in A. Audol
lent, Defixionum Tabellae, nos. 241-242 (see Commentary, below, on

## 第297页

272 52 An Angelic Hierarchy
lines 2ff.; cf. R. Wunsch in Antike Fluchtafeln [Bonn, 1912 = Kleine
Texte], Nr. 3-4). In no. 242, in particular, we find a list of 'angelic'
deities that, like many such lists of angels, presupposes a systematic
etymology to their names (e.g., line Sf.: opKitw ae Tov Oeov Twv ixvip.wv
wt 1rvevp.chwv Am'A.ap.); here, however, the deities adjured (e~opKi!w)
are often 'heavenly,' and not just the usual chthonic, underworld gods
associated with curse-tablets.
The type of invocation that identifies spheres of influence over
which angels sit (o wO~p.evoc;) - a hallmark of our text - represents a
broad, and no doubt long-standing, tradition in Jewish angelic lore. It is
also common to numerous Christian magic and mystical texts which no
doubt derive from earlier precursors. The popular traditions, as
exemplified by our text, generally attest to two closely related hierarchical
systems: First, we encounter catalogues of angels whose spheres of ruler
ship are identified with each of the seven (or more) heavens. These
represent little more than lists, such as "the angel of the first heaven is x,
the angel of the second heaven, is y," and so forth (cf., e.g., lines 15-33,
of this tablet). Secondly, we have lists of angels who are specifically
identified as exercising authority over special natural or geographical
phenomena (c f. lines 34-70). Somewhat related to these (but not occurring
in our text as such) are angels who are given authority over specific dis
eases or afflictions. Here the angel is often etymologically identified with
the particular disease over which he exercises control.
Representative examples of such angelic hierarchies attested
primarily in apocryphal or pseudepigraphic literary sources include, but
are not restricted to, the following:
1) 3 Enoch 14:3ff. (in Hebrew), ed. H. Odeberg, p. 37f. A descrip
tion of angels ruling over various natural elements, planets, and spheres.
2) Gospel of Bartholomew (3rd cent. A.D.; ed. N. Bonwetsch). This
text gives a numerical hierarchy in a summary form. Beliar (alias
Satanael), the first-created 'fallen angel,' is describing in IV. 29 how the
'hierarchy' of angels was first created:
ihr'Aauev 8~-t6 1rpwrov,

The author of the Gospel of Bartholomew is citing fragments from a
larger catalogue of angels, a catalogue that explains their spheres of
influence, and is choosing rather selectively merely to illustrate his
knowledge of the subject before continuing in his narrative. That the
source from which the author of Gospel of Bartholomew ultimately derives
his material is the same as that which is used by the compilers of the
magical literature is clearly demonstrable from the example in IV.45,
given above. It reads in full:

The arrangement of the text of our silver lamella, on the whole, also
indicates the use of divergent, often competing, sources and formulas. The
following outline divides the text of the spell into its larger, discernible
units. The diversity of, and vacillations between, the various expressions
and indeed the different deities and afflictions addressed in the invoca-

## 第301页

276 52 An Angelic Hierarchy
tions, speak for the spell's long, convoluted textual history and for a com
plexity of source-materials used. Noteworthy is the peculiar switching
between singular and plural imperatives as various invocative and protec
tive formulas are applied to whatever issue at hand or diety involved.
Particulars of some of these matters are addressed in the Commentary.

1 opKirw ae: This common adjuration-formula is followed by a verb
of protection in line 6 (fnacf>u'Aa~ov) - a phenomenon comparatively
uncommon on protective amulets, since (e~)opKirew is regularly used of
compelling netherworld demons to perform aggressive or malicious tasks;
or, in protective contexts, of actual 'exorcisms' of possessing demons. It
is also worthwhile to observe in this text the juxtaposing of adjuration
formulas (opKirw ae) with invocation formulas (s7rtm'AoiJJ-tm), as noted in
the outline above.
The construction opKirw ae rov (sc. Oeov) is ambiguous: It can mean
either, "I adjure you, by the god ... ; " or, "I adjure you, the god ... , " as
W. Brashear, inter alia, has observed: see W. Brashear, Magica Varia
(Papyrologica Bruxellensia 25; Bruxelles, 1991 ), p. 51, with references.
For the former of the two interpretations, one may compare, notably,
Mark 5:7' where the demoniac adjures Jesus, by God, opKirw ae 1"0V Oeov,
1-'~ J-te {3aaavianc;. Either interpretation here poses problems: if ae does
not equal rov ( = God), then to whom does the pronoun ae refer? On the

## 第307页

282 52 An Angelic Hierarchy
other hand, if ae is the (Jewish) God being adjured ( = Sabaoth), it proves
difficult to reconcile this with the notion that God himself can be the
object of an adjuration; it is usually a lleKvbo:iJLWII, or a possessing demon,
that is adjured, by God- either to perform a task (if a nekydaimon), or
to flee (if a demon); seeR. Kotansky, "Greek Exorcistic Amulets," in P.
Mirecki & M. Meyer, edd., Magic in the Ancient World (Fortress Press,
forthcoming). This is an adjuration-formula taken from a Jewish exorcism
in which an unnamed 'demon' (ae) is adjured by God to depart. Such
'imprecise' uses of e~opKirw-formulas are rather frequent in the magic
literature, and this seems to be what is partly reflected in lines 79f. and
line 91, below, where 8-Ir<~>opKirw (JB TOll rwiiTOI Oeoll ... <PiryeTe, "I
adjure you (sing.), by the living God ... flee (pl.!)," seems to preserve a
garbled exorcistic formula using a singular pronoun and a plural impera
tive. In the list of To11's that follow in lines 1-6, it is equally confusing
whether it is one god who is being adjured as superior to the other two, or
whether three deities are being invoked individually.

42-43 TOP e'll"t TAI'fAAIQ .. IAOI: mt<c;> TA~.JpNAOT Jordan.
Only the sequence -INAOT- is questionable. The iota is not very plausible
since there seems to be part of a top-to-bottom vertical visible, followed
by what seems to be a 'u'-shaped letter written over a tiny alpha/delta, or
vice-versa. Furthermore, there seems to be a iota, not upsilon, at the end.
Jordan's critical apparatus correctly adjusts for possible variants in giving
f!.L or ~; !1 or p: vA.mc; INAOT for '1Poou H(eron de Villefosse). In his
Commentary Jordan further writes that these letters "must conceal some
phenomenon appropriate in this sequence. I have considered Tote; voam or
voamP and N < i > A.ou for NeiA.ou ... " (p. 67). This may, indeed, have
been the original reading; however, de Villefosse's vA.mc; is supported in
part by a previously unnoticed parallel in the 'angelic hierarchy' of Cod.
Parisi nus 2316 ( "Amulett "), ed. R. Reitzenstein, Poimandres (Leipzig,
1904), p. 297, cited above. There the very phrase in question appears in a
similarly corrupt form: Ea,ura~A. e'll"t Tote vA.oLc {vA.OLc (cod.).
Reitzenstein suggests the restoration e'll"t T~<; VATJ<; ~vA.wP. Jordan's e'll"t
Tote; voam NeiA.ou still seems the most attractive solution. At least the
Paris codex 2316 supports the contention that all these archangelic texts
must have been somehow related. The sequence vA.oLc EvA.oLc certainly
reads like one word glossing another.

80 Tov t[w]vm 8sov iv Za9p9(3sp. vop.aowv Zap.aowv : iv Zaf!!Pf!l
(3sp. Nap.aowv ?-ap.aowv Jordan. "The living God in Zoar of the nomadic

E. Peterson, Eli: 9EOE (Gottingen, 1926), p. 42f.; Preisendanz,
Byzantinisch-Neugriechisches Jahrbuch 3 (1922), p. 279, line 18 (cited
above, on line 1). Does the whole refer rather to Numidians (of Zama?).
The locative, iv, identifies the following as a place-name: Zaf!!pf!!,
or Za9p9 (here with plural terminative -bim) is Zoara (~1Y1T), i.e. Zoar,
the famous Biblical city, later occupied by the Nabataeans, that was
located at the southern tip of the Dead Sea, as described in Josephus
(Ant.J. 1.204: Zwwp; 13.397: Zoapa [some MSS. Zapa] 14.18: Zwapa);
see, further, A. Dietrich, art. Zoapa, RE 2.Reihe, 9. Halbbd. [1972]; M.
C. Astour, art. "Zoar," Anchor Bible Dictionary, VI (1992), p. 1107. The
city is now attested in the Babatha archive (P. Yadin) uncovered from the
so-called 'Cave of Letters.' G. W. Bowersock, Roman Arabia (Cam
bridge/London, 1983), p. 77, n.5, discusses the location and uncertain
orthography of the city Zoara. For the Babatha archive, see N. Lewis,
ed., The Documents from the Bar Kokhba Period in the Cave of Letters.
Greek Papyri (Jerusalem, 1989), esp. p. 21 (Greek spelling = 1rsp't
Zoapa, 7rsp[ Zoopwv, etc.).
The reading vop.aowv, "nomads," is here new, as is the interpreta
tion that Zap.aowv refers to the Za(3aowv, the Zabadeans. The Zabadeans
were an Arabic tribe whom Jonathan the Hasmonean (143 B.C.) invaded,
according to 1 Mace. 12:31£.: m[ i~iK'Awsv lwva8av e1ri TOuc; "Apa(3ac;

## 第318页

52 An Angelic Hierarchy 293
Toile; w"Aovp.ivovc; Zaf3aoaiovc Kat B7C'cY7a~8JI avTOilc; Kat e'Aa(3ev Ta
(JKVACX CXVTWJI. Kat avafeu~ac; ~Mev elc; t:..ap.aO"KCJJI Kat OLW08V(J8JI BJI 7C'cYO"T/
Tfi xwpa. Josephus, Antiq. 13.179, refers to the same event, but it is
believed that he is in error here, for he refers to Nabataeans of Arabia,
not Zabadeans of Arabia in describing Jonathan's campaign: 7C'O~TJO"ap.evoc;
o&v eKe'iOev T~v V7C'OO"Tpocf>~v elc; T~v 'Apa(3iav, Kat 1ro"Aep.~O"ac; Toile;
Naf3aTnvouc, KTA. But scholars are wholly unsure of the exact location of
the Zabadeans; A. Kasher in the article, "Zabadeans," in the Anchor Bible
Dictionary, VI (1992), p. 1030f. (cf. his Jews, Idumaeans, and Ancient
Arabs [Tiibingen, 1988], pp. 137-141) believes that 1 Mace. 12:31f. sug
gests the region of Damascus. But the reference says nothing of the
Zabadeans' base of operation, nor of the breadth of their territorial
domain; in fact, it only mentions that Jonathan brought the Zabadeans
captive to Damascus. This notice, on the contrary, might support the
opposite view: Jonathan would hardly have led the Zabadeans in captivity
to, or near, a city of their domain. Other references to place names in
'zbd' in the Damascus area may also be purely circumstantial.
In any event, either our text is also confusing Zabadeans with
Nabataeans, as Josephus did, or there is more to be said for the view that
the Zabadeans were not territorially distant from the Nabateans. The city
of Zoar, in the southern-most tip of the Valley of Jericho, was taken over
by the Nabateans in Hellenistic times, from whom Alexander Jannaeus
conquered it; seeM. C. Astour, art. "Zoar," in The Anchor Bible Diction
ary, VI (1992), p. 1107 (with refs.).

Since these allusions to underground 'founts of the Abyss' are all

about the source of the Nile in subterranean, watery depths. If so, this
would support all the more that the origin of this text as a whole is to be
sought in Egypt (cf. further, Commentary, lines 99, 102, below).
95-97 Zva f.l.~ (3A.a7rTT/TB f.l.~re f.i.oA.vvT/re ~ ¢apf.1.aKwanTe auT~< , > :
LSJ s. v. ¢cxpf.1.cxKow, gives for the passive, "to be poisoned;" "to be

magic and poisoning must have been common, day-to-day concerns in the
ancient world, judging from the following questions and answers
preserved in the Sortes Astrampsychi (ed. G. M. Browne):
(Question-format):

The noun a'll'avT~ ( = a'll'avnwu;), "chance meeting," is practically
unknown apart from the LXX, where it occurs some 26 times in the
phrase eic;; a'll'avT~v ( = 11'1i?). This noun-form in our text seems to point
again to a dialectal usage perhaps local to Alexandria, a usage conceivably
reflected independently in both the LXX and in our text. (It is not likely
that the reading of our silver tablet derives directly from the LXX, since
LXX always uses the 'transliterated' phrase eic;; a'll'avT~v). Does the single
occurrence of the word a'll'aVT~ in Recension C of the Testament of
Solomon (C IX, 4; McCown, p. 78*) point to an Alexandrian origin of
the Testament, as well? (On the Testament's origin, see D. C. Duling,
Journal for the Study of the Pseudepigrapha 2 [1988], p. 96f.).

a'1!'av7~ ( = a'll'aVT'Y/CJLc;;), could quite possibly represent idiosyncratic

## 第323页

298 52 An Angelic Hierarchy
'Alexandrian' versions of otherwise common counterparts - they are
calques, as it were, that probably have their origin in the peculiar Jewish
bilingual community of Egypt. The occurrence of such terms in
Alexandrian contexts (e.g. in the LXX and the Alexandrian Church
Fathers) can be explained as deriving from a common local usage, rather
than from borrowing directly from the LXX. The magical papyri always
use the very common owouaia (PGM 1.31f.,42,291; IV.898; Xlc.8;
XIXa.54; Suppl. Mag. I, no.38,12; 45,7,31) but never auvouataaJJ.{)(;.

## 第326页

53 Christian Amulet for Ophthalmia
Fig. 53
TYRUS? (Tyre), Lebanon Late Roman/Early Byzantine?
Berlin, Bodesmuseum (?) H.3.4 em.; W. 3.4 em.
no inv. number Gold lamella
Lit. M. Siebourg, "Ein griechisch-christliches Goldamulett gegen Augenkranken
heiten," Bonner Jahrbiicher 118 (1909), pp. 158-175; R. Kotansky, in C. A. Faraone &
D. Obbink, Magika Hi era (Oxford, 1991 ), p. 120.
This amulet was acquired in 1904 from a coin-dealer in Frankfurt
who had obtained the piece in Tyre. Although its exact provenance is not
stated, the piece no doubt came from a nearby tomb in the Syrian area.
Ancient Tyre is as good a candidate as any for the amulet's Fundort, since
the town "embraced Christianity enthusiastically" - holding a bishopric
as early as the 2nd century- and was site of an enormous necropolis: W.
L. Macdonald, art. "Tyrus," in R. Stillwell, ed. The Princeton
Encyclopedia of Classical Sites (Princeton, 1976), p. 944.
The gold lamella preserves an unusual Christian amulet. The text
opens with a Christian trinitarian formula - a formula which, according
to its original editor, is to be dated to the 1st century A.D. Unfortunately,
no facsimile or photograph of the piece was ever produced, by means of
which a date could be more securely assigned, palaeographically (we
reproduce here only Siebourg's diplomatic transcript). Siebourg was also
mislead in thinking there was a iota-adscript in line 8, an apparent mis
reading that would have influenced his theory of an early dating. The text
is probably late (Ill-Y A.D.), though there may be some indications that,
as far as Christian amulets go, it ought to be assigned to the earlier end of
this chronological scale: late Christian amulets regularly cite the Psalms,
the Gospel of Matthew or John, or allude to specific miracles of Christ;
none of these features is found in our text, which rather contains a string
of unusual magic names (Aramaic?), including the common lao. There is
also no mention of the bearer's name.

The trinitarian formula given here, though, is noteworthy. Although
none of the expressions by themselves seems odd, the precise grouping,

4 'Pa~aaKaVOJl.Ka: It is not clear how these names are to be
divided. Brockelmann apud Siebourg 1909:161 has suggested underlying
Aramaic and Semitic: pa~a is Aramaic M:J,, "the great;" awv is
Babylonian sakan (BH, C'.llC), "governor, head;" and OJl.Ka is Aramaic,
"the Deep;" cf. MR~~Y. cumqti', "depth," BH p~·y, comeq (and cognates).
The whole might then be 'the great governor of the Deep.' Perhaps
closer, though, is Po/. sal5en, "inhabitant" (Is. 33:24; from Sfsn, "to
dwell"). And the position of M:J,, too, suggests a predicate: 'Great is the
dweller of the deep.' But a~aawvoJl. - despite its Aramaic potential -
may simply represent a misreading of a~aawvov (vel sim.), "free from
envy;" "free of the Evil Eye." A reference to the evil-eye would be
particularly fitting in a spell dealing with eye-complaints (ophthalmia).
5-7 "Aov"Aa CXJl.PtKropaOr-,v aOa~aOa povpaK: Again, Brockelmann

follows: "Loula-amri, gebunden die Augen deine Tochter des Fiebers. "
The linguistic particulars of this interpretation are difficult to evaluate,
since the division between the words here presents an unusually large
number of possibilities. There is also a particularly wide range of possible
Semitic roots with which to compare each potential syllable. Aov)\a, for
example, can be interpretated as containing an imperative, "come!" (cui)
C'W+,,
or as some form of the verb such as infinitive ZCwl). The same
letters can be read as "evil-doer," or "nursling," and so on. The whole
could also be a part of the preceding w of line 4. However, )\ou)\a is

9-11 T~l' B1fLc/>epop.ivYJI' ocf>8a'Ap.i(!v: Almost identical terminology is
used to describe the symptoms of bilious ophthalmia in Theophilus
Protospatharius, De corporis hwrumi fabrica libri v. (ed. Greenhill), p.
164, 9f., EYJp.e'ia xo'Awbov~ o¢8a'Ap.iac. llYJAOt be TO DCXKI'WDB~ elvm Kat
Dptp.v TO B1fL¢epop.evov TOL~ op.p.wn, KTA., "The symptoms of bilious
ophthalmia: the onset is a pain and piercing in the eyes," etc.
The sense of e7rtc/>epop.evov as the onsetting or onslaught of a disease
(that is, one that is "incipient," "impending," or "threatening") is captured
in the Latin transliteration of e7rtcf>opa in Marcellus Empiricus, cited by
Siebourg 1909:167: Gramen, quod in summo trisulcum habebit, descres
cente luna radicitus sublatum quam plurimum repone, deinde ex muftis
unum auspicante lippitudine ad collum subliga, celeriter incumbentem
epiphoram discuties (Marcellus Empiricus 8.24). On this Siebourg rightly
observes, "Hier steht also die auspicans lippitudo 'die beginnende
Triefiiugigkeit' synonym mit der incumbens epiphora, wobei das incubens
genau unserm e7rtc/>epop.ivYJ entspricht und die Grundbedeutung von
i1rtcf>opa so verwischt ist, dass die Tautologie nicht empfunden wird." It is
further worthwhile to note that e7rtc/>opa (from which our verb
e7rtc/>epop.evo~ derives), is used as a technical term, epiphora - a
'persistent flow' of tears (cf. LSJ, s.v.).
Other Christian spells for eye-diseases occur in Daniel &
Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. I, nos. 26 and 32; cf. also the Ripe San Ginesio
gold amulet (our 31).
11-13 p.YJ[Ki]n iaan~ ocf>8a'Ap.[iad !,LI'' iv{3o['A~v ')'Bvea8m]: An
example of a similar p.YJKin-formula is given in Daniel & Maltomini,
Suppl. Mag. I, no. 25, 6: p.YJKin &1/;n TV 1/;vxn Tov cf>opovvTo~ (with com
mentary); here, however, God himself is petitioned to thwart the
enchroachment of the disease. Medically, ip.{3a'A'Aw (and presumably the
noun ip./3o'A~) means "putting in." Here, it is used of affliction.

London, The British Museum gr. H. 7.0 em; W. 6.4 em.
Cat. no. 3151 Silver lamella
Lit. F. H. Marshall, Catalogue of the Jewellry, Greek, Roman, Etruscan, in the
Department of Antiquities, British Museum (London, 1911 ), p. 378, no. 3151.
Marshall's editio princeps provides the following description of this
piece: "Seven fragments of a thin silver plate with Gnostic inscription
intersected by lines . . . from Haifa, Syria. Presented by Miss Constance
M. Swan, 1900. Ca 3rd cent. A. D." Though providing a drawing of the
piece, Marshall printed no actual readings, since the text's condition
affords little to work with. Here, however, we have been able to read
some sequences of Greek and provide a first edition of sorts.
The text, though clearly magical as the few voces magicae show, is
not a conventional amulet. The few glimpses of good Greek show, rather,
a text that is an astrological fragment, part of which seems to be hom
scopic or prognostic. It also seems that certain planetary alignments are
being judged favorable for uttering magic incantations. For the nexus, cf.
PGM VII.284-299 (KvKAoc; EeA~V'T/<;), a text that indicates among other
things, the favorable constellations in which incantations are to be per
formed. The relevant lines read: ro~orrr 1rpoc; ~Awv mL aeA~V'T/V
e7rtKA'T/(JLV ijrot e7rLACXA~JUXTO!, KTA. "In Sagittarius: (make) an invocation
or incantations to Helios and Selene (sun and moon)," etc. Note also, in
general, PGMIV.835-849 (Commentary, below, line 6).
Portions of our amulet seem to have been separated into compart
ments or sections by long ruling lines, which may serve to delineate to the
reader separate astrological texts or prognostications. There is also a
diaeresis on iota (line 7), dicola (lines 2, 7), regular iota adscriptum (lines
2,7,8,9), and sigla (end of lines 2, 6). It is not certain how much of the
text on the left margin is lost, nor in fact how much space is to be allowed
between the separate fragments.

Near Haifa (Mt. Carmel), Israel III-IV A.D.?
Paris, Musee du Louvre H. 4.6 em.; W. 3.3.
Bj 85 (lnv. M.N.C. 2145) Gold lamella
Lit. A. Dain, Inscriptions grecques du Musee du Louvre. Les texts inedits (Paris,
1933), p. 177f. no. 203 (with drawing).

## 第337页

56 A Jewish Liturgical Fever-Amulet
Fig. 56. Plate XXI
Kh. Mu.sli~ (near Kibbutz 'Evron), Israel III-IV A.D.
Israel Antiquities Authority H 4.3 em; L 7.8 em
IAA No. 88-193 Copper lamella
Lit. R. Kotansky, "An Inscribed Copper Amulet from 'Evron," 'Atiqot 20 (1991),
pp. 81-87.
This small copper amulet was found in 1988 by a kibbutz member
while he was conducting casual surface explorations at the ancient site of
Kh. Mu.sli~ (geographical map reference 1621/2656). Though the 15-acre
site, located 1 km. east of 'Evron in Western Galilee, has never been
excavated, the area preserves scattered remains of viticultural structures,
tiles, potsherds, and tombs dating from the Persian through Byzantine
periods; see Kotansky 1991:81; C. R. Conder & H. H. Kitchener, The
Survey of Western Palestine I (London, 1881; repr. 1970), p. 180. In the
editio princeps, the amulet was dated to the 4th-5th century A.D. Now a
3rd (or 4th) A. D. seems preferable, based on closer scrutiny of the letter
forms.
The text preserves a Greek fever-amulet (or prayer) largely com
posed of Jewish liturgical language, based on the LXX. The opening lines
contain an invocation of Divine Names, however, in Hebrew. Although at
times grammatically peculiar, the Greek is largely that of one who wrote
down Biblical verses as if from memory, or as if Greek were his second
language. Much of the amulet's phrasing, on the other hand, is also found
in Greek medical writers. Whether this can be explained as evidence of a
direct knowledge of such sources is not certain. Coincidence provides the
least likely explanation for the common vocabulary. The writer of our
therapeutic prayer may indeed be directly borrowing from recommenda
tions found in contemporary medical diagnosis. Given the liturgical nature
of much of this spell, this language may simply have originated in formal
prayers to be spoken over the sick - prayers whose nearest counterparts
are found in the later Christian Euchologia. The expressions that our

## 第338页

56 Jewish Fever Amulet 313
tablet shares in common with medical writers further suggest that its text
derives only indirectly from the tradition of the Greek magical handbooks.
Perhaps specialized healing rituals, developed for cultic community needs
and imbedded within a set liturgy, can best explain the rationale behind
the writing of a charm such as this. The rituals would betray, as the
language of the tablet itself indicates, a more focused concern for con
valesence and healing through 'orthodox' religious prayer, rather than
through the more forbidden, medico-magical practices with which these
lamellae are so generally associated. Like the later attested Christian
evxai for the sick (discussed in connection with several of the lamellae in
this corpus), our text can in no wise be read as a 'magic incantation,' in
the traditional sense of the word. The words it preserves betray no trace of
the usual magic names or formulas. There is little, if anything, on it that
could be misrepresented as 'pagan' or 'unorthodox.' Not only is there not
a single vox magica, 'Abrasax,' or 'Ablanathanalba,' the whole is devoid
of the Jewish angel-names so often found on the emphatically Jewish
examples of these amulets. The only divine name permitted this text is
that of the one Hebrew God, lao Sabaoth.
The text of our amulet, then, must have arisen out of a formal set
ting of some kind. The use of healing rituals and the engraving of 'magic'
amulets seems to have been a by-product of the more sectarian of the
Jewish synagogues, and it is perhaps here that we should look for the
development of such liturgical incantations among Hellenistic Jews. Heal
ing rituals and other more arcane rites associated with the dead have also
been attached to a number of ancient synagogal practices (see, in general,
Shaye J. D. Cohen, "Pagan and Christian Evidence on the Ancient
Synagogue," in Lee I. Levine, ed. The Synagogue in Late Antiquity
[Philadelphia, 1987], pp. 159-181; esp. pp. 165-169; cf. also Com
mentaries on 2, 52, etc.). And, indeed, a number of Aramaic amulets
preserving prayers and incantations on gold and silver lamellae have
actually been recovered from ancient synagogues in Nirim (the north
western Negev). Some of these have actually been excavated from near or
in the Holy Ark of the Covenant (see J. Naveh & S. Shaked, Amulets and
Magic Bowls, Leiden & Jerusalem, 1985, p. 37f.; p. 90f., and passim).

## 第339页

'Holy is the Lord!' May the God himself who by his Word created all things,

who made everything, lao Sabaoth.
Hebrew section (lines 1-4):
The names of God found m these lines are widely attested on
Aramaic amulets and bowls (cf. J. Naveh & S. Shaked, Amulets and
Magic Bowls [Jerusalem & Leiden, 1985], pp. 280-82) and require little

## 第340页

56 Jewish Fever Amulet 315
comment. The use of Hebrew at the beginning serves as an opemng
invocation, occupying a position usually reserved for the prestigious
angel-names, voces magicae, or magical xapaKrf,per;. Although it plainly
does not represent the more expressive portion of the spell, the citation of
the nomina sacra, for the scribe and his client, sits well within the
boundaries of acceptable orthodoxy. As mentioned above, no intrusive
deities such as, Abrasax, Marmaraoth - or even angel-names - appear
on this tablet. Despite the syncretistic character of these amulets, here the
writer sustains a strict monotheism throughout his text.

The doctrine of God's creative word, of central importance to our
amulet, is based upon several Old Testament references. Besides the
standard account of creation in Genesis 1:3-6, etc., the following provide
additional comparanda: Psalm 32:6 (LXX): TW 'Aoyw TOV KVp[ov oi ovpavo(

In magical texts the god who creates (Kriretv) the universe is also widely
attested: PGM III.554 (o Ta ?rai'TOi KTLCJOi<;); IV.1709 (opK[rw ... KOit TOP
1ravm Kriaavm; cf. below, line 12f.); XIII.63 (ra 1ravm Kriaavm; cf.
XIII. 983); etc.
The AD"(oc; by which God created the Universe refers in part to the
Hebrew incantation at the beginning of the spell; it reflects the belief that
God made the world by a mystic utterance, a mystic utterance identical
with his own Ineffable Name; see, e.g., J. Trachtenberg, Jewish Magic
and Superstition. A Study in Folk Religion New York, 1939; repr. 1979),
pp. 82, 90, 288 (n. 8), 289 (n. 26); E. E. Urbach, The Sages. Their Con

discussed below, gives the imperative owp,am. Does this late example go
back to an original optative?

The import of the preceding lines, as suggested above, is that just as
God's word creates, it also heals. Here we are not dealing so much with
the Word qua healing-incantation - for this, the arcane Hebrew of lines
1-4 would have been more appropriate - but rather with the notion that
God's utterance (M'Yod carries power in itself to heal. Cf. Ps. 106:20
(LXX): orrriaT8LA81' TOll AO'}'OI' O!VTOV Kat iaaaTO avTOV~' Kat eppvaaTO

a
M. Sartre, Rostra. Des origines /'Islam (Paris, 1985), p. 209, s.v., cites
occurrences of Kaa(a)w~ (particularly in Syria) and suggests its
popularity can be explained by 1) children being named after the Syrian
legate under Marcus Aurelius, Avidius Cassius; and by 2) a Greek render
ing of a regional Semitic name, ':lj:', ("remove; set aside," etc.; Sartre,
loc. cit., translates "eloigne"). Though F. T. Gignac, Greek Grammar of
the Papyri I (Milan, 1976), p. 158f. gives numerous examples of a> aa,

## 第345页

320 56 Jewish Fever Amulet
one wonders whether the spelling Kaaaw~ ( = Latin Cassius) here is to
be assumed at all. A more likely explanation for the name is to be located
in a toponym: the famous mountain, Kaaw~, south of Seleuceia Pieria,
had been long venerated for the local Semitic divinity (identified with
Zev~ Kaaw~) believed to reside there and honoured with annual festivals.
See A. B. Cook, Zeus. A Study in Ancient Religion, II (Cambridge,
1925), pp. 981-983; idem, III/ii (addenda), p. 1177, with copious notes
on mythology, history, and etymology; further, B. V. Head, Historia
Numorum. A Manual of Greek Numismatics (London, 1887; repr. 1977),
p. 783 (for coins). Kasios can thus be best explained as a Semitic name. A
possible etymology is 'C:l, i1C:l, "to cover;" "hide, conceal;" and
"forgive" (Psalm 32:1).
MrJrpaownov (-oorwv): The a is unmistakable, so a name m
MrJrpQ- is unlikely. A form of Metradotion (nor even of MetrQdotion) is
not otherwise attested. The 'name' here beginning p.~rpa-, "womb," fol
lows the pattern of other pseudonyms found in this position: PGM
XXXII.11: Eapa1naoa ijv 8reKev eiOi<;x l!iJ.w..g ( = XXIIa.5); PGM
XVI.6,15,22, etc.: Eapa1riwv ov 8reKe Ilaaaumpa (Preisendanz,'s
articulation is mistaken. Read 1raaa p.~rpa !). See D. R. Jordan, "CIL VIII
19525(B).2 QPVVLVA = q(uem) p(eperit) vulva," Philologus 120 (1976),
pp. 120-132. Thus, the mother's name, Metradotion, does not represent a
genuine personal name, but a filler-name ( = "given-by-the-womb") sup
plied for the missing mother's real name. The parallel texts prove that
magical formularies used p.~rpa-phrases to indicate where the name of a
client's mother ought to be inserted when it was not otherwise known:
Mnrpaoonov belongs in the same category as the other pseudo-names
I&aJJ11..11l_Q, Ilaaa~, and Vulvasua (see Jordan, op. cit.).

The added ending, Tov 1rm~aavm 1ravm (along with its initial
adjuration opKirw ek), is not part of any Biblical quotation. It reads rather
(as much of this text) like something from an original confessional or
credal formula. A similar formula occurs in the two epitomes to Clement
of Rome (ed. Dressel): Clemens Romanus, Epitome prior 42, llf. ( =
Epitome altera, 42, llf. ): 1rL(JT8U8LII ~ eva Oeov TOll 1rOLVaav7a 1rcXIITCX,
Ta Te opaTa KCXL Ta aopam. The only other occurrence of TOll 1rOL~(J(XIITCX
1ravm among Patristic or Biblical writers is that found in the Martyrium
Pionii, 19.8 (ed. H. Musurillo, The Acts of the Christian Martyrs,
Oxford, 1972), p.160: B1r1]PWT1]ae· IIoia~ Beoae{jeia~; Ct1r8KpiB1]· Til~~
Tov Oeov 1raTipa Tov 7roLnaavTa Ta 1ravTa. Both of these citations form
part of a confession or creed, and both use ei~, as our text does; the
'Evron tablet, however, uses ek as an adjuration formula, not as a state
ment of faith: opKirw ek, "I adjure by ... , " or, "I swear by ... ". It seems
that our 'citation' stems from a similar liturgical context as that found in
Clement and the Acts of the Martyrs, but our formula carries a specifi
cally exorcistic application. Very similar formulas also occur with opKirw
ek - used of adjuring demons - in A. Vassiliev, Anecdota Graeco-

(MPG, vol. 43), p. 493,56; p. 496,40: TOV T~V )'~V eopa(JCXVTa.
15 'law I;a{3aw0: These names of God ( = rm~:Jl i11i1') are common
in magic texts as voces magicae but in these lines represent names of the
Jewish God, as Creator. On the names, in general, see 32,29, etc.

Lit. P.Perdrizet, "Amulette grecque trouvee en Syrie," Revue des Etudes Grecques

1 [e7rLK]a;>..oup.m: For this hymnic invocation on magic lamellae, see
52,13, etc. The long list of magic deities in lines 1-10 then were not only
written on the tablet but also probably invoked aloud. The address in line
12 to the "lords, archangels, gods, and divine charakteres" in the vocative
represents the prayer proper to be fulfilled.
1-3 'law, Mtxa~"A ra{3pt~A Ov[p]t~"A: These first four names form
a Tetrad, with lao standing in the place of the usual Raphael; see 26, with
Commentary.
3 'Ap{3a0ww · Ap{3a0taw: This name (='Fourfold lao,' a reference
to the Tetragrammaton) is discussed fully in W. Fauth, "Arbath Jao. Zur
mystischen Vierheit in griechischen und koptischen Zaubertexten und in
gnostischen und apokryphen Schriften des christlichen Orients," Oriens
Christianus 67 (1983), pp. 65-103; cf. 2,4.
7 Aaa)\ap.: The name corresponds to Hebrew or Aramaic C'Y',
"forever," itself a variant of the more common "Aa'i"Aap.. The diaeresis
found in some manuscripts (like the 'Great Paris' magical papyrus) sup
ports a pronunciation approximating the supposed Hebrew; cf. PGM
