## 第356页

58 Victory Charm before the Governor of Arabia
Fig. 57. Plates XXII & XXIII
Bostra, (Arabia), Jordan IV A.D.
Walters Art Gallery, Baltimore H. 6.9; W. 4.3 em.
Inv. no. 57.1960 Gold lamella
Lit. R. Kotansky, "Magic in the Court of the Governor of Arabia," Zeitschrift for
Papyrologie und Epigraphik 88 (1991 ), pp. 41-60.
This text preserves a victory-charm (vtKYJTLKiw) - more precisely, a
vtKYJTLKov Kat. OvJJ.OKI.hoxov - a spell that combines requests for benefits
(lines 12f.; 37f.; 43f.) with curses and imprecations against the opponents
(26f.). Written to secure a victory in a trial before the military governor
(dux) and his adsessor at Bostra in Arabia, our amulet must have come
from somewhere near that city and not from Egypt, as reported in the
Walters Art Gallery records. The naming of the two officials in our text
adds considerably to the prosopographic record of Roman Arabia in the
period of the 4th century. More on the identity and chronological place
ment of these officials- both new- can be found in the editio princeps.
Furthermore, the use of legal nomenclature with the titles and names
of governor's office suggests that the scribe who wrote our spell may have
been a court-clerk who had access to magical books containing
formularies. M. W. C. Hassall, in W. Rodwell, ed. Temples, Churches,
and Religion in Roman Britain, vol. i (British Archaeological Reports,
British Series 77; Oxford, 1980), observes certain legal terms on Latin
curse tablets and concludes that the "legalistic jargon of many of them ...
strongly suggest[s] that they were written by professional scribes, who
knew how to impress credulous clients" (p. 87); cf. D. R. Jordan, "New
Defixiones from Carthage," in: J.H. Humphrey, ed., The Circus and a
Byzantine Cemetery at Carthage, vol. I (Ann Arbor, 1988), p. 125);
further, Campbell Bonner, "Witchcraft in the Lecture Room of Libanius,"
Transactions and Proceedings of the American Philological Association 63
(1932), pp. 34-44, esp. pp. 42f., who similarly notes the apparently com
mon use of scribes to write magic charms: in Orat. 1.62-63, Libanius tells
how he and his {3t{3'Aw-ypa¢oc; were indicted on charges of magic by a

## 第357页

332 58 Victory Charm
rival sophist who wished to blame the orator for his wife's death by
insanity; see, text, translation, and commentary in A.F. Norman,
Libanius' Autobiography (Oration I) (Oxford, 1965), pp. 38-41, 164f.
(magic signs) law,

(1) (magic signs) lao [Ad]onai esaaaaa (magic signs) Sabaoth eeeee eeeee
(magic signs) phphph mchchps Astrael ee ooo )YO (magic signs) lao Sabaoth
Amiopheoueuisagizouera ee iouo (magic signs) Chenmo Zemia ThoptiechO
BadetophOth Batho (magic signs) lao Sabaoth Ia ipioth z th. (11) Holy and
strong, mighty and great-powerful Name, give favor, glory, victory to
Proclus whom Salvina bore, before Diogenianus the dux of Bostra in Arabia;
before Pelagius the assessor; and before all men small and great; before
gods; before daimons, in order that he might be justifiably or unjustifiably
victorious in any judgment before any judge, before a magister, before all
who observe him and who hear him and his words.
(22) Establish him strong. Make (him) a victor over everyone, particularly
over Salvina, whom 'Eve' bore, who bore Abdancha, Balona, Mikke; in
order that Proclus, whom Salvina bore, might receive victory and glory.
(26) Silence, subjugate, and enslave Salvina, whom 'Eve' bore; and Abdan
cha and Balona and Mikke, whom Salvina bore; and all enemies, opponents,
and associates; and Diogenianus the ruler; and Pelagius his scholasticus, an
assessor; and all opposing parties, small and great; men, women and
children - all who are watching Proclus, whom Salvina bore, the one bear
ing your holy and unconquerable power.
(37) And give (him) power, victory, favor, courage, boldness, (raising) awe
in everyone, in all fortune and life, in every place, according to every hour,
both day and night.
(41) And may no one act in opposition, or speak in opposition, or deliberate
in opposition in anything at all except for the victory, favor, glory, power
for the one bearing your glory; now, now; quickly, quickly. Amen!

334 58 Victory Charm
1-11 Magic signs (xapaK;ijpe~) are a common feature of magic
texts and often occur, as here, occasionally interspersed with nomina
sacra and voces magicae. On 'Iaw, Ea{3aw0 'Aowvai in lines 1,2,3,6, and
10, see, e.g. 12,14 and 'Index V,' svv.
'ACJ;pafA: Evidently formed from the Greek aCJ;pa plus -'Y]A: cf.
'ACJTPO!'Y/A(or;) in PGMXXXVI. 258, and 'ACYrpa.e"A on a magic nail from
Rome (Bulllnst, 1849, p. 11).
Fig. 57

## 第360页

58 Victory Charm 335
7 wvw: A plausible cipher of the Tetragrammaton. The chi-rho
monogram, as well as the 'Amen' at the end (line 45), and possibly the
use of 'Eve' (cf. 28), are the only possible 'Christian' features of this
spell, which is otherwise marked by the usual syncretistic elements. By
the time of the writing of this spell, Christianity had already been the offi
cial state-religion for some 40-50 years; occasional Christian elements
should come as no surprise.
8 'Aprev: cf. PGM IV.6,715. This is interpreted by K. Fr. W.
Schmidt, Berliner Philologische Wochenschrift 41-42 (1935), p. 1183, as
Egyptian irw-tnj, "erhabenes Geschopf"
9 XOevp.w: cf. PGM XIXa. 3 xOevOe{3evx.

IX.3f. ({3ab71bo</>w), Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. II, no. 53,10
([{3a]D71ro<f>w0t). The name is spelled with either delta or zeta.

The text begins by naming those highest on the socio-political lad
der: the governor and his legal adviser; e1rt with the genitive here means
"in the presence of;" "before" a magistrate (see LSJ s.v., def. 1.2.e).
Though Proclus is to appear before the governor, his real opponents are
the litigants themselves (lines 23-25, 28-30, 31-35). The legal dispute
must have been of some importance, since pettier cases would have been
delegated to lower judges (Cod. Theod. 1.16,8; 1.29.2; A. H. M. Jones,
The Later Roman Empire I, Oxford, 1973, pp. 479f.; 481). Lawsuits were
to be tried before governors of the province in which the defendant
resided (Cod. Theod. II.1,4; 1.22,2). Furthermore, as Jones remarks,
"Claims for property ... seem normally to have come before the provin
cial governor" - an indication that Proclus' trial, too, may have been
concerned with property matters (Jones, The Later Roman Empire, I, p.
486; II, p. 1206, note 35). See further, on lines 19, 24f.
In naming a presiding governor and his assistant, our gold leaf com
pares with a lead defixio written to silence Theodorus, the governor of
Kourion on Cyprus, as well as a certain Timon: Ke Me; c/>tJ.Lov r[;,J eeoowp4.J
r[;,J ~')'8[J.L011( rijc; e1rapxeiac; ? I Kv1rpou KB TiJ.LWII( (cf. lines 8, 19): T. B.
Mitford, The Inscriptions of Kourion (Philadelphia, 1971), no. 130, pp.
253-255 (cf. no. 131, p. 257); A. Audollent, Defixionum Tabellae (Paris,
1904), no. 25.

The term O"V"fKaOeopoc; is generally thought to render Latin assessor
(cf. Mason, Greek Terms, p. 88, s.v. O"V"fKaOeopoc;, adsessor, coadjutor);
however, it is relatively uncommon, occurring only in late patristic writers
and grammarians; rarely on inscriptions; only once on an ostrakon (SB
2253, 13 = Shelton, ed. Ashm. G. 0. 160; see below); and only once on a
papyrus (P. Oxy. 50.3570.3). An inspection of these texts suggests that a
O"V"fKaOeopoc; was a judicial colleague of high rank being attached
generally to a provincial governor's office - a fact supported by our text
as well. For example, 0. Ashm. Shelton, 80 (John C. Shelton, ed. Greek
Ostraca in the Ashmolean Museum = Papyrologica Florentina 17;
Florence, 1988) mentions in a list of food-deliveries, a 1rpivKtif; and a
avvKaOeopoc; who receive double the meat ration that their subordinates
get. Shelton (p. 67) observes:
The titles if not the functions of these persons obviously show strong Latin
influence, but the only real clue as to whether the background is military or
civilian is the presence of an assessor. The latter must have been a legal
adviser who assisted some official of such importance that he was entitled to
preside at a court of law. The only other O'V')'Ka8c&por; known from Egypt
occurs in P. Oxy. L 3570.3 (AD 285), where the man was attached to an
epistrategus. That could also be the case here, or some other official, such as
a praeses, could be involved.
These observations are confirmed by other occurrences of
O"V"fKaOeopoc; on inscnptlons and in literature: An epithet from
Kolybrassos in Pamphylia (4th cent. A.D.) mentions Konon as
av"fKaOeopoc; to the governor of the Thebaid: avvKaOeopov ei]{317c;
Net"Awtooc; ~"fBJ.l.OV~oc; (G. E. Bean & T. B. Mitford, Journeys in Rough
Cilicia, 1964-1968 [Wien, 1970], no. 49, pp. 74-77; cf. J. F. Gilliam,
Zeitschrift jar Papyrologie und Epigraphik 13 [1974], pp. 147-150). An
inscription from the reign of Septimius Severns (C IL VIII .1640 =

NLK7JT~c; is never found in Greek magic. The title was an imperial
epithet popularized by Constantine in lieu of avtK7JToc; (invictus) - a title
that carried associations with Sol invictus, and hence was inappropriate for
a Christian emperor. In literature VLK7JT~c; is almost exclusively confined to
4th century Patristic writers who use it either in reference to Constantine
or in the sense of a spiritual victor (which does not apply here); cf.
Eusebius, De laud. Const. 111.3: VLK7JT~V avrov mBtarac; exBpwv
Cx7rcXVTWV KaL 7rOAep.iwv; idem, X. 7: Cx7rcXVTWV exBpwv KaL 1rOA8p.iwv
VLK'YJT~V avrov mBiaT7Jatv; G. Rosch, ONOMA BAEIAEIAE. Studien zum
offiziellen Gebrauch der Kaisertitel in spiitantiker und fruhbyzantinischer
Zeit (Wien, 1978), pp. 45f., 78f. NtK1JT~c; (victor) in our text is probably
a borrowing of contemporary imperial titulature (cf. aviK7Jroc; [=invictus]
in line 37).

oavxa, Ba"Awva< v>, MtKK7JV: Our text names Salvina as Proclus' chief

= Littmann, et al., Greek and Latin Inscriptions in Syria [Part 5; Leyden,
1915, etc.], p. 334, with discussion); A{3oa"A"Aac;, 'servant of Allah,'
(Princeton Exp. Inscr. 46, 11-Umta'iyeh, Syria). The accusative ending in
-a, if Greek, tells nothing about the sex of its bearer, but other theophoric

31 KCiL dW')'BVWVOV TOV apxovm: Here Diogenianus is named
apxwv in addition to his title oov~ in line 14 above. Though the use of
apxwv for a civil governor is quite common by Justinian's time (cf. R. E.
Briinnow & A. von Domaszewski, Die Provincia Arabia, Bd. 3,
[Strassburg, 1909], p. 281), it certainly occurs earlier on inscriptions and
in literature, though the formal contours of the title are imprecise. Greek
writers (as opposed to stone-engravers) use apxwv as a general name for
'governor' with little regard for its formal nomenclature. Libanius, for
example, in describing governors of Arabia contemporary with our
Diogenianus, regularly uses the term apxwv; see Epist. 320, 329, 337,
357, 358 (of Maximus, ca. 357-358 A.D.); Epist. 763 (of Belaeus, ca.
361-363 A.D.), etc.; cf. Mason, Greek Terms, pp. 110-113; esp. p. 112,
who remarks: "... apxwv is not used in formal inscriptions and official
titulature to designate the praeses provinciae." The title cannot be used
with any precision for purposes of dating. Nevertheless, according to
Sartre, the title apxwv is first attested as a designation for a governor of
Arabia, Flavius Anastasius, on an inscription from Qasr al-Hallabat in 529
A. D., referred to as b p.e')'etAo7rpe7riam7o~ KetL ivoo~om7o~ Kop.rJ~, oov~
KCXL apxwv, 7o {3'. As dux he exercises his capacity as military governor;
as apxwv ( = iudex) his capacity as civil governor (so Sartre, Trois etudes,
pp. 108f., §80). However, already in ca. 343-348 A.D., as Sartre (Trois
etudes, p. 10 2) notes, the title apxwv 7~~ i1rapxia~ is used of an Arabian
governor, Flavius Antonius Hierocles; cf. Lallemand, L 'administration
civile, p. 60: "Le terme imprecis d' apxwv qui, dans les papyrus, designe

## 第369页

344 58 Victory Charm
frequemment les gouverneurs a partir du Ve siecle, s'emploie deja au
milieu du IVe" (with references).
We must consider the use of apxwv here in two possible usages,
which may not be mutually exclusive: 1) = iudex, that is Diogenianus is
designated as a civil governor in his capacity as judge; and 2) = provin
cial governor. Our text is not a public document, but a private magical
text; formal nomenclature would not be expected.
31-33 KO!t IIeA.a'Ywv, rov <J)(oA.aanKov avrou, awKaOeopov: As the
province's principal legal officer, Pelagius must have been of some rank,
for he is both assessor and scholasticus. Here only are the two terms
apparently used in reference to the same person. On our text, the term is
distinguished from the more common <J)(oA.aanKf)(; (see lines 32f.).
Though the <J)(oA.aanKcJI; designates a mere advocate, here it refers to the
advocatus praesidis; see A. Claus, "' 0 ExoA.aanK{)(;," (Inaug. Diss.
Koln, 1965), esp. pp. 132-148: "Die <J)(oA.aanKoi an der Seite hoherer
Beamter," and pp. 20-42 for list (with titles) from the papyri and inscrip
tions, supplemented by P. J. Sijpesteijn, Zeitschrift for Papyrologie und
Epigraphik 70 (1987), pp. 143-146; and T. Gagos, Zeitschrift for
Papyrologie und Epigraphik 79 (1989), p. 272; see further, R. Pintaudi &
P. J. Sijpesteijn, Tavolette lignee e cerate da varie collezioni
(Papyrologica Florentina 18; Florence, 1989) on 2.2 (p. 23); H. G. Ioan
nidou on P. Oxy. LIX. 4002 (p. 166, n. 23, with further discussion). On
advocates in general, see R. Taubenschlag, The Law of Greco-Roman
Egypt in the Light of the Papyri (New York, 1944), pp. 386-88.
For the later use of <JX.OAaanKfH; is designate the civil function of a
governor himself, see the discussion in editio princeps. The scholasticus,
Pelagius, is no mere lawyer: he is a powerful administrative officer in
charge of the governor's civil affairs. But our text belongs to an earlier
period when the function of the scholasticus was kept separate from that
of the dux, even though our governor, Diogenianus, exercises both
military and civil duties. This represents a transitional period when the
civil and military duties of government were being invested into a single
office.

## 第372页

59 A Fever Amulet
Fig 58. Plate XXIV
Oxyrhynchus, Egypt III A.D.
Cologne, Institut fiir Altertumskunde H. 9.0; W. 1.5 x 3.5 em.
T. Colon. inv. 7 Silver alloy
Lit. R. Daniel & F. Maltomini, Supplementum Magicum Vol. I (Suppl. Mag. 1),
(Papyrologica Coloniensia XVI.2; Opladen, 1990), pp. 7-10, no. 2; D. Wortmann,
"Neue magische Texte," Bonner Jahrbiicher 168 (1968), p.l 04, no. 6.
Daniel & Maltomini 1990:7-10 now give the preferred reading of
this fever amulet, first published by Wortmann 1968 and identified as
lead. From autopsy, it appears that the piece is certainly not lead;
however, it is also not pure silver, but probably a silver alloy debased
with lead or tin. The faint, tiny ringlet texturing of the surface clearing
indicates the process of annealing (not "decorated with small emboss
ments," as indicated in Daniel & Maltomini 1990:7). The piece is roughly
triangular or cone-shaped with the bottom broken off.

evbo~ov ( ± ovoJ.LaTo<;) > 0(3ax Kai KTA. cannot be ruled out in light of

Daniel & Maltomini's brave attempt to wrest any sense of it (" ... the one

4-7 K<Xt m(r a) TOV {3a{3apa(}av {3apox 'A{3p<XCXJ.I. aa{3apaaJJ.: Daniel

explained by K. F. W. Schmidt, GOttingische gelehrte Anzeigen 196
(1934), p. 175 as Egyptian c3-b3gj. w, "Great One of the Dead," or
"Door-guardian of the Dead;" or as Coptic, "to be white; shining" (see
Suppl. Mag. I, p. 9, acknowledging H. J. Thissen). If the former, this
certainly belongs - as the context of Suppl. Mag. I, no. 50 suggests - to
the realm of aggressive, and not protective, magic. It is important to note,
in support of this, that the magic names of the Kara rov {3ap{3apa(}av +
aa{3apaaJJ. formula are found almost entirely in binding love-spells that
adjure (i~opKi(w!) underworld daimons for the aggressive acquisition of
love. Note the 'long form' of the spell in Suppl. Mag.!, no. 46,15-18:

Our text then, though a protective amulet, shows conclusively the

has also turned the usual {3apovxap.{3pa of the aggressive spells into an

Jewish exorcism that here also borrows from the netherworld adjuration of
a nekydaimon (e~opKitw ae oa'ip.ov, oanc; 1ro7' o&v). The meaning of the
names as a group can be located in Semitic cognates referring to "break
ing," "smashing;" "fracture," "calamity," and "destruction"- direct
references to earthquake deities and the activities they cause. Cf. Aramaic
and Hebrew ,~~. sabar, "to break, break up" (Qal); "mash, shatter"
(Piel); ,~W, seber, "destruction." Ea{3apaap. quite possibly combines the
cognate shr with (M)CY, "crowd, people" = "(He who) smashes/destroys
(the) people." (The ending -aap. could also go back to i17~iJ. "to roar;
rumble"). The parallel name, Ea{3ap{3aptaw0, cited above also suitable
combines ,::1~ with rnM',:l ( = BaptawO), "creations, formations," plural
of :11$''1~, bar'i'ah, "creation, formation" (i.e., of heaven and earth).
Hence aa{3ap{3apww8 represents '(He who) breaks creations/foundations'
- another earthquake deity.
Such etymologies fit admirably in the contexts of the adjuration
formulas that also speak of splitting open the earth, the sea, founts of the

Fig. 58

## 第378页

60 A XaptT~UtoP
Fig. 59. Plate XXV
Oxyrhynchus, Egypt II-III A.D.
Cologne, Institut flir Altertumskunde H. 4.7 em.; W. 5.2 em.
P. Colon. inv. T. 8 Silver lamella
Lit. R. Daniel & F. Maltomini, Supplementum Magicum Vol. II (Suppl. Mag. II)
(Papyrologica Coloniensia XVI.2; Opladen, 1992), p. 66f., no. 64; D. Wortmann,
"Neue magische Texte," Bonnerlahrbiicher 168 (1968), p. 106, Nr. 8.
Wortmann first published this text in 1968 without photograph or
drawing. Daniel in Daniel & Maltomini: 1992 gives an improved reading
and correctly redated the piece from the sixth century A.D. (Wortmann)
to the second or third. Wortmann also misidentified the material as lead.
A still newer reading, given below, differs in some details from that given
in Daniel & Maltomini 1991:66. We also publish a full commentary and
facsimile - one made from a tracing of the actual tablet (scale, 1: 1, here
reduced to 90% ).
As Daniel (1992:66) observes, the amulet is a conventional
xaptr~awv. Examples of such 'favor' charms are rare among the corpus
of gold and silver lamellae. Another in this corpus (40), written on gold,
contains a list of 'virtues' not unlike that found here. Silver, or gold, must
have been considered particularly suitable for writing xaptr~aw. This
proves all the more noteworthy when we observe that T. Colon. 8 comes
from Oxyrhynchus, a town known for its plentiful supply of papyrus.
Hence, we cannot avoid the assumption that the writer of our spell was
following a formulary, such as that given in PGM XXXVI.35-68, where a
xaptr~awv is to be written on a silver leaf ("AaJlVCX ap"fvpfx); see, further,
discussion below on lines 3-5. In antiquity, the engraved tablet was folded
several times along its vertical axis from right to left. Accordingly, the
letters on the left - though this innermost margin remained intact - are
corroded and sometimes illegible. The frayed right margin has
deteriorated, resulting in the loss of ca. 2 letters.

## 第379页

354 60 A xcxpLnjowv

Daniel. The text here is so difficult that one is forced to introduce some

Excursus: "To Grant i1ra¢poowia before the King"
The presence of i1ra¢pof>taia (4f.), "loveliness, elegance" (LSJ),
among the list of virtues of our spell - and in the parallel examples from

## 第382页

357
the magical papyri adduced below - leads us directly back to a distinctive
'blessing' -formula attested in the Ptolemaic papyri of the 3rd century B.C.
Our Greek e1rcx</>poouricx, it can be shown, is actually a calque on an
Egyptian idiom of long pedigree. The bearing that this has on the develop
ment of favor and victory charms warrants a closer look at this relatively
uncommon noun.
Before turning to an examination of the noun in the papyri, a brief
survey of 81rcx</>poowicx in Greek literature is in order. Apart from the
documentary Ptolemaic papyri discussed below, it is remarkable that this
noun is not attested in Greek literature before the second century A.D.
Indeed, its use - at least among Greek authors- is apparently limited to
that century and to that century only (pace LSJ, s. v., in reference to Lyn
ceus, ap. Athen. 6.242C, where no actual fragment exists: see Kassel
Austin, PCG V, p. 616f.). Among Greek authors, the noun is only found
in Appianus of Alexandria, Athenaeus of Naucratis, Artemidorus,
Favorinus, Ptolemaeus, and Vettius Valens. Three of these writers happen
to come from Egypt (Appianus, Athenaeus, and Ptolemaeus) and two
derive their source-material from Egyptian traditional beliefs (i.e., Vettius
Valen's astrology and Artemidorus' Oneirocritica). Statistically, Appianus
of Alexandria uses e1rcx</>poowicx only once (ap. Suida, s.v. e7rcx</>po&ro<;);
Athenaeus of Naucratis, twice; Artemidorus, once; Ptolemaeus, once
(Tetr. 86); and Favorinus in [Dio Chrysostom], Orat XXXVII.33, only
once. Vettius Valens uses the term considerably more than the rest (7x).
The relative frequency with which we encounter the word in Vettius is due
to a repeated use of the word in descriptions of astrologically favorable
conditions (see ed. Pingree, index verborum, s.v.).
Clearly the noun is uncommon. But is it merely a coincidence that
81rcx</>poouricx' s attestation in Greek literature is restricted to the 2nd cent.
A.D.? Does its use by so small a small group of writers say anything
about the noun's limited circulation and its possible geographical origin?
Although there is little reason not to think that e1rcx</>poowicx could have
enjoyed a wide geographical use throughout the early Roman period, it is
remarkable to observe how meagerly attested it actually is in our sources.
A look at the papyri from Egypt, however, shows a quite different
story: a remarkable number of occurrences of e1rcx</>poouricx, including that

## 第383页

358 60 A xaptrijuwv
of our tablet, can be found in documents of Egyptian provenance. Most
important of these is its presence in the famous Zenon archives, dating to
the 3rd century B.C.! There the noun occurs in a formal context that can
best be described as a royal blessing, a type of invocation that looks sur
prisingly close to what we find on our later Greek charitesia-spells. In
Ptolemaic Egypt we are to look for the possible coinage of the word.
P. Zenon Pestm. 50, line Sf. [ = PSI IV. 328], preserves a letter
(dated 9 January, 257 B.C.) from priests of Aphrodite to Apollonios, con
cerning the mummification of a sacred cow called Hesis. The relevant
lines, at the end Of the letter, read aS follOWS: f'LVWO"K8 OB elvciL T~JI 'tatJI
Elaw (XVT~ Oi (JOL ooin e7ra¢poowiav '!I"P[O~ TOJI {3am]~ia ... , "Know
that the Hesis is Isis: may she grant you favour [before the king.]" (tr. W.
J. Tait in P. W. Pestman, ed. Greek and Demotic Texts from the Zenon
Archive (P.L. Bat. 20), Leiden 1980, p. 191). The editor's excellent com
mentary on these lines is worth citing in full:

... iw=w di n=k ~s.t mr.t S'w fm-b3~1 Pr-'3 Sbk ntr "3' s'tjt

the great god unto eternity.

on naturally as a pendant to the name of a god.
The other examples of the Ptolemaic formula, mentioned by Tait,
extend this sort of virtue list to include 'virtues' other than e1ra</>poowia:

## 第384页

60 A xapLTljOWP 359
It seems, then, that e1ra¢po&aia was apparently restricted in Classi
cal and Hellenistic Greek to a special blessing-formula attested among the
Graeco-Demotic bilingual populace of Ptolemaic Egypt. The noun is
nowhere else attested in the Greek prose or poetry of the time, nor does it
even appear outside of Egypt until the 2nd century A.D. But the term is
widely found in the Greek magical papyri -texts that are themselves to
be dated throughout the Roman period, and texts that of course derive
from Egypt.
In particular, the texts of the Zenon archive compare quite favorably
with the late magical spells, both as to the use of e1ra¢poowia and as to
the overall context in which the prayer-like formulations occur. These
Ptolemaic 'blessing' -formulas can certainly be seen as possible prototypes
or models for the later magical xapLT~ma. Not only do the Ptolemaic and
late-magical 'blessing' formulas share the same 'Greco-Demotic' term,
e1ra¢poowia, both categories make their requests using some form of
oiowJu. They also itemize lists of virtues that closely match: in addition to
e1ra¢pooLaia, each of the nouns xapL~, p.op¢~, evrJp.epia, and evrvxia, of
lists in the Zenon archive is also found in the equivalent magical
xapLT~ma: p.op¢~, for example, occurs in the very long list at PGM
III.576-581: tw~v, iryeiav, aWTTJpiav, 1r"Aovrov, evreKviav, "fVWmv,
8VCLKofav, evp.ivewv, ev{3ov"Aiav, 8VOO~LCLV, JJ.V~p.'f'JV, xapLV, !).OpcbrJV
(579), Ka"A"Ao~; cf. PGM VIII.27,31: 00~ JJ.OL T~V xapLV, p.op¢~v, KaAAO~
(where Ka"A"Ao~, as above, must surely translate the same Demotic lying
behind e1ra¢poowia). Cf. also evp.op¢ia in Suppl. Mag II, 63,14-16: 06~
JJ.OL VLKTJ[V], oo~av, evp.op¢iav 7rpo~ 7raVTCi~ KCi~ 7rpo~ 7ra!J"~~. So, too,
evrJp.epia is attested in the list at PGM VIII.5, cited above. The noun
xapL~, as mentioned, is commonplace in such lists.
Here is a list of the occurrences of e1ra¢poowia in the Greek magi
cal papyri. The noun always occurs in the context of 'Favor and Victory
Charms:'

To conclude, it would not be difficult to hypothesize that the
Graeco-Egyptian xapLr~cnov of the magical papyri represents the direct
and natural outgrowth of an earlier prayer, or blessing formula, in which
favor and loveliness before the Pharaoh was invoked. The presence of
i7rm/>poowia - an apparent calque on Demotic mr.t - undeniably
connects the later magical xapLrrwia with their Ptolemaic counterparts
and accounts for the unusually wide proliferation of the term in these
'favor' charms. The 'well-wishing' expressed between one party to
another in the Ptolemaic formulas is, of course, replaced in the later magi
cal invocations by direct, personal requests to the gods themselves. So
also the general social context for the use of the two blessing-formulas
differs widely. The magical versions have certainly become more com
mercialized and opportunistic. Further, the magical texts ask for xapu;
"before all men and women," not usually before a Pharaoh or one of the
Ptolemies. That this feature, however, still survived in the xapLT~aw of
the later magical papyri can be seen in the frequent application of these
spells before kings and rulers (PGM XII.201-260; XII.270-350;
XXXVI.35-37; and XIII.250).

## 第386页

61 A Nubian Conception and Fertility Charm
Fig. 60. Plate XXVI
Ballana, Nubia III-IV A.D.
Cairo, Archaeological Museum H. 6.5 em.; W. 3.5 em.
Inv. J. 71204 Gold lamella
Lit. S. Eitrem in: Walter B. Emery & K. P. Kirwan, The Royal Tombs of Ballana
and Qustul, I (Cairo, 1938), p. 382 (Cat. No. 874): pp. 405-407; Pl. 107B; R. Daniel &
F. Maltomini, Supplementum Magicum Vol. II (Suppl. Mag. II) (Papyrologica
Coloniensia XVI.2; Opladen, 1992), p. 157f.; cf. Jean-Jacques Aubert, "Threatened
Wombs: Aspects of Ancient Uterine Magic," Greek, Roman, and Byzantine Studies 30
(1989), pp. 421-449, esp. p. 427, n. 8.
This inscribed sheet of gold, first published by Eitrem in 1938, was
found with three lead scrolls (that have never been unrolled) in Tomb 2,
Cemetery 219, at Ball ana. A small gold cross and scarab ring were also
found in the grave. The Museum registry on 'Sec V-8' reads "#11474 Pla
que, bearing an incised Greek inscription III-IVP gold .. 098 x 0.55 Bal
lana 1931-1933. Tomb no. 2."
In September, 1989, Mrs. Elham Montasser, first curator of the
Egyptian Museum, kindly granted me permission to re-examine the piece
in Cabinet 36 (Room 44), whence it was removed for the first time in
nearly sixty years. A fresh examination of the tablet itself and of prints
later provided from the Museum's photographic department have made an
improved reading possible. Some, but not all, of these new readings were
provisionally reported in Suppl. Mag. II, p. 158.
Eitrem presented the text with little commentary, stating simply: "A
very interesting love charm, an Agage [sic], with a peculiar utilization of
Osiris' 'living water', i.e. semen (Egyptian origin)" (1938:405). The spell
is addressed to a woman, identified mythically as Isis, to come to a man,
named as Isis' brother (and hence her husband), Osiris, who offers her the
'fertile' waters of Horus, Ibis, and Anubis. References to the opening of
the womb and the reception of the sown waters indicates that the charm's
aim is fertility and conception, and not an a')'W')'~, as Eitrem suggests.

one with normalized spelling. The phonetic interchange between ad8,

also a regular metathesis of u8pw for u8wp (cf. E. Mayser, Grammatik der
griechischen Papyri, I [2nd ed. Berlin, 1970], 189). Diaeresis and colons
are used, and there is some evidence of a corrector's hand.

Tr. Come to me, Isis because I am Osiris your male brother. These are the
waters that I offer you: it is the water of the Falcon [Horus]. (that) of the
little breasts of Ibis, (and) the water of Anubis. Brother Phthnerh ben. Be on
your back (and) open your womb in this hour, in this moment, and receive
quickly the water sown in you, in your name Isis, mistress, queen of
Denderah; now, quickly, quickly; through your power, quickly.

"Pudenda key spell: Take an egg of a crow and the juice of the plant
crow's-foot and gall of a river electric eel, and grind them with honey and
say I the spell whenever you grind and whenever you smear it on your geni
tals.
This is the spell that is to be spoken: 'I say to you, womb of NN, open
and receive the seed of NN and the uncontrollable seed of the IARPHE
ARPHE (write it). Let her, NN, love me for all her time as Isis loved Osiris
and let her remain chaste for me as Penelope did of Odysseus. I And do you,
womb, remember me for all the time of my life, because I am
AKARNACHTHAS.'
Say this while grinding and whenever you rub your genitals, and in this
way have intercourse with the woman you wish, and she will love you alone
and by no one else will she ever be laid, just by you alone." (Tr. E. N.
O'Neil in Betz, Greek Magical Papyri in Translation, p. 276).
The spell's invocation mentions the opening and receiving of the
germinating seed much in the same way as does the text of our gold leaf.
Similarly, we find allusions to the love of Isis and Osiris. Spells for con
ception were common in the magic of both the Egyptian and Roman
periods; cf. e.g. PDM xiv.428-50; 1046-1055, in H. D. Betz, Greek Magi

and the thorough treatment in Aubert 1989:421-449, cited above; further,
the two short recipes in Daniel & Maltomini, Supp l. Mag. II, no. 79,1-
11, "Two formulas to be recited by a man after copulation so that a
woman conceives," which name a~a, not vowp, as the impregnating sub
stance.

The noun aoeA.4>ov in line 9 plays no syntactical role in the sentence
and sharply contrasts with the peculiar aomp4>o~ and other spellings found

## 第392页

61 Nubian Fertility Charm 367
throughout this spell. The orthographically accurate aoe"A¢ov may
represent a corrector's attempt to replace the unsound phonetics of
aowp¢oaov in line 3. The reading got falsely inserted at line 9, in addi
tion to, rather than replacing, the reading of line 3 (see Commentary,
above, on line 3).
In Grenier's documentation of literary and epigraphic records, there
is no explicit reference to the 'water of Anubis,' although Anubian water
containers (Anubis Hydreios), or depictions thereof-akin to the more com
mon Osiris Hydreios statues-have been uncovered throughout the Mediter
ranean; see R. A. Wild, Water in the Cultic Worship of Isis, pp. 113-118
(see above), who connects these Hydreia with other known symbols of
Osiris' association with water (cultic water pitchers, and the so-called
'Cool Water' inscriptions), suggesting that they represent the promise of
life beyond the grave (cf. Wild, op. cit., pp. 126-128). Although Wild
does not go into detail about the few instances when Anubis Hydreios
types are found in lieu of the usual Osiris Hydreios-types (specifically,
Wild, op. cit., pp. 114, 118, 245, n. 113), it seems clear that Anubis
shared with Osiris some cultic association with water. In our text, one can
imagine that Anubis as the embalmer god, also attends on Osiris'
revivification by bringing him life-giving water, and that the 'Anubian
water' - like that of the Ibis - symbolizes life, fecundity, and
procreativity.

as a putative reference to Nubia itself, the find-spot of this amulet: since
Old Kingdom times nhsjw, "Southerners," became a general designation
for the land of Nubia (see St. Wenig, art. "Nubien," Lexikon der
Agyptologie 4 [1982], col. 526). The south (Thebes and beyond) was also
popularly believed to be the land of magicians.

## 第394页

62 A Magic 'Sword'
Fig. 61
Zian, Tunisia II-III A.D.
Paris, Bibliotheque Nationale H. 7.0 em.; W. 4.3 em.
Cabinet des Medailles Collection Froehner, no. 285a Gold lamella
Lit. S. Reinach & E. Babelon, "Recherches archeologiques en Tunisie (1883-
1884)," Bulletin Archeologique (1886), pp. 56-57 (fig. 12).
A drawing of the reverse side of this tiny gold lamella was
published in Reinach-Babelon 1886:56f. with no transcription. The text
contains mostly magic names and symbols, but is of some interest: it
comes from an undisputed Fundort; preserves a version of the same
xapaKrijpe~ and magic names found at the beginning of our 57 (from
Bostra, Arabia); and contains a notable reference to love-magic. The text,
written in and around a drawing of a Roman gladius, preserves in its
handle the Greek command, Jlaivotro, "Pray she go mad," a phrase often
used of driving a woman to sexual frenzy. The image of the sword
provides a good parallel the so-called 'Sword of Dardanos' of the magic
papyn.
The gold lamella was found below the forum at Zian in the bottom
of a shaft connected to a triangular underground vault. The shaft was used
as a refuse heap, having been filled with gravel and remains of human
bones. The very bottom of the excavated shaft yielded discarded frag
ments of inscriptions, bronze sculpture, and the gold amulet, herewith
presented.
The text is divided into an upper-register, written horizontally across
the tablet, and a lower section with the letters written vertically above and
below the inscribed sword.

1-7 The series of characters and names here is virtually identical
with that of the Bostra gold lamella (57). The variants on a common
angel-name in both tablets requires further comment. The text of 57,5
gives a single angel-name 'AaTpa~'A (followied by TIT/ oo uuw) - a read
ing that parallels line 7 here, in the sequence wtaTpa.,'Aw. The natural

## 第396页

62 Magic 'Sword' 371
conclusion would be to interpret the name here as w 'larpa~A., m (or, (;)
'larpa~A.) - that is, a common variant of the angel-name, 'lapa~A.. Both
readings, 57 and here in 62, seem to derive from a common textual
ancestor, but which is closer to the original? The name here on 62, even
though it is apparently linked to the spell's function, may point rather to
an original ((;)) 'Iarpa~A., a reading that may lurk behind 'Aarpa~A. in
57,5 as well. Nevertheless, as shown in lines 29-31 below, the Zian
lamella is a love-charm. We interpret accordingly the sequence of letters
in line 7 as wtarpa (read otarpa), "sting her to sexual madness." The
command then parallels that found in the 'sword's' handle: J.taivotro,
"Pray she go sexually mad!"
The self-same imperative, otarpa, occurs as the only word on an
unpublished silver phylactery that has engraved above it the facing heads
of a man and a woman. The verb is common in sexual magic; the form
oiarpwJ.tivq (varpwJ.tivrw) occurs in Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. I,
no. 38,9f.; PGM IV.2910 gives </nADT1Jn KQ(L evvfl, Ot<7Tpi.J) eA.aUVOJ.tBI'TJI'.
An overview of the texts of actual defixiones would considerably increase
this list. The imagery is that of the stinging gadfly, but in magical con
texts is always used of sexual madness and frenzy - specifically "any
vehement desire; insane passion" (LSJ, s.v. 11.2 olarpo~).
As a sort of angelic personification, however, it is still possible to
read this as the name Oiarpa~A. - not, however, as 'lstrael,' but rather
as Oistrael, the angel of 'sexual frenzy' (see discussion below).
The 'Sword': The use of an inscribed sword is particularly
appropriate in love-magic, as the famous 'Sword of Dardanos' of the
Great Paris Magical Papyrus (PGM IV .1716-1870) shows. There, follow
ing instructions on engraving a special stone of Aphrodite and Psyche with
a long invocation (lines 1716-1811), directions for making a 'sword' of a
gold lamella are described (1812-1829):

372 62 Magic 'Sword'

"Sword of Dardanos: Rite which is called 'sword', which has no equal bec
ause of its power, for it immediately bends and attracts the soul of whomever
you wish ... And on a golden leaf inscribe this sword: 'One THOURIEL
MICHAEL GABRIEL OURIEL MISAEL IRRAEL ISTRAEL: May it be a
propitious day for this name and for me who know it and am wearing it. I
summon the immortal and infallible strength of God. Grant me the submiss
sion of every soul for which I have called upon you.' Give the leaf to a
partridge to gulp down and kill it. Then pick it up and wear it around your
neck after inserting into the strip the herb called 'boy love'" (tr., E. N.
O'Neil, in Betz, Greek Magical Papyri in Translation, p. 71).
The instructions to write the 'Sword' on a gold leaf compares aptly
with our text, but here there is a list of actual angel-names to be written
onto the gold leaf. The leaf (and the spell) is called ~ic/>oc; because it was
to be shaped like a sword, enclosed in a drawing of a sword, or otherwise
arranged in such a shape (the technique is called technopaignion); cf. K.
Preisendanz, art. "Xiphos," in W. H. Roscher, Lexikon der griechischen
und romischen Mythologie VI, col. 527; and the classic study of R.
Mouterde, Le Glaive de Dardanos, Melanges de l'Universite Saint-Joseph
15/3 (1930), p. 105f., no. 33 (Pl. 11,5; fig. 33). Our lamella indicates
how the practice was so interpreted in antiquity.
The presence of o'Carpa (in wiarpet7JA) on our tablet also shows a
close connection with the tradition of the 'Sword' of Dardanos. There we
find a group of angel-names to be written on the gold leaf, of which one
happens to be the same or close to ours, namely 'Iarpa~"A. The name, in
its resemblance to our wwrpet7JA, may indicate a remote textual
relationship of some kind between the two texts, for the 'Sword of Dar
danos,' also includes forms of oiarp&v. For example, line 1761 reads, KCJ.L
CJKOTewov ep.1rviwv olarpov, "... who breathes dark frenzy;" and line
1777f. gives the hapax legomenon, oiarpo)'eviTwp, "begetter of sexual
frenzy."
In the handle of the sword one appropriately reads p.ivod)w (read
p.aivmro), "may she be driven mad." Again, the term is common in
sexual contexts; cf., e.g., PGM IV. 2756: p.awop.iv7J ~ o(e'iva) ijKOL e1r'
ep.a'im Ovpmm raxwm, "In frenzy may she (N) come fast to my doors"

## 第398页

62 Magic 'Sword' 373
(tr. O'Neil in Betz, Greek Magical Papyri,2 p. 90); and esp. Daniel &
Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. II, no. 72, i, 10 (for the exact form here): 1ravra.
V7r8p88JLSVT/ gaivoLTO e7r' BJL~L q>LADTT/TL, "setting everything else aside,
may she fall madly in love with me" (p. 109).
In later magic, we may add, the so-called 'Sword of Moses' was
also a name given to an apparently popular magic recipe; see M. Gaster,
Studies and Texts in Folklore, Magic, Medieval Romance, Hebrew
Apocrypha and Samaritan Archaeology I (London, 1925), pp. 288-337.
The 'Sword' in this instance refers to magic names of God to be engraved
or used in any number of magical operations. Similarly, the medieval
Clavicula Salomonis gives the name 'Sword' to a magical treatise: see the
introduction in H. Gollancz, ;,~'?tu ;"11'1!>~, Clavicula Salomonis. A Hebrew
Manuscript. (London and Frankfurt, 1903). A late magic treatise in A.
Delatte, Anecdota Atheniensia I (Paris & Liege, 1927) refers repeatedly to
the Jl.cXXCXLpa r~~ rixv11~, a sword engraved with magic names and used
for various magic operations (pp. 12,19f.; p. 13,2; p. 14,9,20; p. 23,15;
p. 24,4; p.25 [drawing]; 41,2; p. 46 [drawing]; p. 406,15-26; etc.).
The three elements our text shares with the traditional 'Sword of
Dardanos' of the magical papyri, viz., an engraved "Sword," the angel
(O)istrael, and forms of oiarp&v, suggest that the Zian lamella belongs to
a larger complex of magical spells having to do with love-magic and
inscribed Dardanian 'swords.' On the name, see now W. Fauth, "Dar
daniel (PGM LXII 12-16)," Zeitschrift for Papyrologie und Epigraphik 98
(1993), pp. 57-75.

## 第399页

63 'Marearmar'
CARTHAGO, Tunisia Roman Period?
Tunis, Musee d' Alaoui H. 3.0 em.; W. 2.3 em.
Sec. H, cat. no. 60 Gold lamella
Lit. L. Drappier in: P. Gauckler & M. L. Poinssot, et al., Catalogue des Musees
et Collections Archeologiques de L 'Algerie et de La Tunisie. Musee Alaoui, Supplement
(Paris, 1907), cinquieme section (E-H) 118, no. 60; A. Audollent, Defixionum Tabellae
(Paris, 1904), p. 359, no. 262.
This lamella was discovered folded up in the sand by the seashore at
Carthage and was briefly described in Audollent's corpus of lead tablets.
The magic word marearmar in the first line may coincidentally allude to
the sea ('mare'), or simply be a variant of the Greek magic name
Mapp.apawO, on which see, e.g 12,13; W. Brashear, Magica Varia
(Brussels, 1991 ), p. 40f. It cannot be determined whether the tablet had
been intentionally cast into the sea - as if some offering to a sea-god
'Marearmar' - or whether the folded piece belongs to some scattered
remains of an original Carthaginian tomb long washed to sea.
1 marearmar (magic sign)
2 (magic signs)
3 (magic signs)
4 (magic signs)
5 (magic signs)

64 Three Divine Angels
THYSDRUS (El Jem), Tunisia Roman?
Present location unknown H. 10.9 em.; W. 7.5 em.
(no inv. number) Silver lamella
Lit. R. Cagnat, "Seance de Ia Commission de I' Afrique du Nord (10 janvier,

1 GABR[IEL],
2 SABAOT-
3 EL, MIKAEL
This tablet, written in Latin characters, was published with a
promise of a fuller treatment latter, a study that apparently never
appeared. No photograph or facsimile was published.
The piece was found among the ruins of stelai dedicated to the god
Saturn. Cagnat 1930:54 gives the following inventory of objects found at
the site, as well as a description of the circumstances of the tablet's dis
covery:
"Trois monnaies en bronze, une en argent, routes puniques. Enfin, des
a
indigenes ont apporte M. Castagne les fragments d'une fine lamelle
d'argent, longue de 0 m. 10, large de 0 m. 075, sur laquelle est inscrit un
texte cursif dont ['intention magique est assez revelee par ['invocation de
demons Gabr[iel], Sabaot, El, Mikael. Ce document aurait ete decouvert
parmi les steles dediees a Saturn; l 'inventeur indigene precise meme qu 'elle
aurait ere plaquee entre les colonnettes dessinees au-dessus de l 'inscription
publiee sous le No. 15."
The inscription no. 15 cited above (Cagnat 1930:50) is itself de
scribed as follows: "15. Sommet de stele; lettres de 0 m.005; calcaire
gris: saturNO MAgno Sacrum. Au sommet de Ia stele est incisee Ia sil
a
houette d'un edicule deux colonnettes aux angles."

## 第401页

376 64 Three Divine Angels
As far as the magic phylacteries of this corpus are concerned, this
report is of some interest, for it records the only instance of such a text
being affixed to a public monument. Most gold and silver phylacteries
served as private amulets and were worn about the body.
We interpret the magic names here, widely known in their Greek
counterparts, as three angel-names in -el (e.g. ra{3pt~A., Ea{3aw(}~A.,
Mtxa~A.), rather than the four given in the editio princeps (i.e., Greek,
ra{3pt~A., Ea{3awe, -;-HA., Mtxa~A.), though both readings are possible.
For an interesting parallel, note Daniel & Maltomini, Suppl. Mag. I, no.
27, lf. HA., HA., HA., b (}eo<; Ea{3awe iilieev roil awam rov aJ.H~P!9?--[Mv.]
(see their Commentary, foe. cit., p. 75, not observing, however, the pos
sible play on words with HA. and Ea{3aw(}-~(}ev.
The magic names inscribed on the silver piece would have been
invoked to protect the sanctuary to which the amulet was affixed, and/or
the text was meant as a prayer directed to the god Saturn's attention.

65 A Gold Amulet for Abbagaza
Fig. 62. Plate XXVII
Kopc-Taly, Feodosija (Theodosia), Crimea II-III A.D.
Paris, Musee du Louvre H. 1.6 em.; W. 6.5 em.
Bj 86 (Inv. M.N.D. 1341) Gold lamella
Lit. V. Skorpil, Zapiski der Odessaer Gesellschaft jar Geschichte und Altertumer
21 (1898), pp. 8-11 [Russ. non vidi]; E. Pridik, 'TpeqecKUI 3aKJiliTI H AMyJieTbi H36
IO~Hoii Poccm," OT!!b KJIACCWIECOtl C/>HJIJIOJIOIH, 1899, no. 7, p. 123f.; A.

Archiv jar Papyrusforschung 11 (1935) p. 159f.
The editio princeps of this amulet (and 65) is Skorpil 1898, whose
text is reproduced in Pridik 1899. Preisendanz 1935:160, though giving
no transcription, provides the bibliographical information on Skorpi1 and
Pridik (whose works are given in German titles). Preisendanz was not
aware of Dain 1933. Dain, who subsequently published both 65 and 66
after they were acquired by the Louvre, was also unaware that both had
been previously published with superior readings. Here we provide new
readings of both 65 and 66 from photographs not previously published.
A shepherd found amulets 65 and 66 rolled together up in a capsule,
in the steppe-lands of the eastern Crimea at Kopc-Taly, Feodosija
(Theodosia). Preisendanz, writing in 1935, claims that the piece is in the
Odessa Museum. Dain, writing in 1933, says that both 65 and 66 were
acquired by the Louvre from the Collection Messaksoudis in April 1920.

The personal names are probably Iranian. Though the text shows Christian
influence (note the cross at the beginning), it oddly invokes the per
sonified Hygeia for healing, a phenomenon uncommon, though not
entirely unexpected, on magic texts such as these.
The imperative of Oepcx1revw used of God who heals sickness
(usually fever in the examples below) is with very few exceptions
restricted to Christian amulets. Imperatives in the pagan examples below
occur twice in the plural, once with infinitive. The plural imperative -
something not usually to be expected of a Christian or Jewish prayer -
does in fact occur in one example below.

1 'OpKitw Vf.i.Cx<;: The plural pronoun refers to the group of afflic
tions in lines 4-6. The opKitw (or i~opKitw) formula appears relatively
rarely on amulets and protective charms unless specifically 'exorcistic';
see 51,1; 52,79; 56,31f.; 68, [1]. The adjuration-formula- common in
aggressive magic for adjuring nekydaimones to work curses and erotic
magic on the practitioner's behalf - carries a somewhat different sense
when used in protective magic: it refers, as here, to the exorcism of an
indwelling demons. Note, for example, the great exorcism of the Paris
Magical Papyrus (PGM IV.3007-3086); further, other 'Solomonic'
exorcisms, or adjurations of original Jewish derivation: Suppl. Mag. I,
rw
no. 24, 3f.' ... 9PKL ae mrg. ... mi EoAOfJ.WV[ ---) ...

scorpions, e.g., Suppl. Mag. II, no. 89, 6,7f.: opKiao, 1r&v aKop1rie, ...
i~op"fWW [sic] a[---]; 2) fever: Suppl. Mag. I, no. 19,14-17: i~opKitw
~f.l.Cx<; ... tva Oepa7revarJre (fever); and, 3) rheum and migraine: Suppl.

(Cherubim, etc.) a1roOepa1revaaTe (pl.!) oci>OaAfJ.OV[!;] a1ro ~fl..~!(pavov,
KTA. But in such cases, internal grammatical incongruities usually betray a
careless combining of i~opKitw-formulas with commoner but diverse -
and often incompatible - prayer-formulas. In at least one instance, we
have an actual 'compulsive' adjuration (of nekydaimones?) fastened to a
command to heal - again, mixing the plural pronoun with the singular
imperative: Suppl. Mag. I, no. 12, 2,5: i~opKiow Vf.i.Cx<; mra r~<; 7r~Kpfxc;

exorcisms. Similar phrasing occurs in the following liturgical exorcisms
attested in several MSS. (important textual variants are marked):
1) An exorcism attributed to John Chrysostom: Supplementum ad
Liturgiam S. Chrysostomi (Migne, PG 64), p. 1061C:
b esoc; b etiWPto<;, b AUTpWC1CtJ1.SPO<; TO )'CPO<; TWP (xp()pw7rWP CK Tflc;
alxJl.aA.wuiac; Toil l'Jta{36A.ou, puum TOP &JuMP uou TOPOS c'x1ro 7rCtCJ11<;
CPsp-ysiac; 1rPSUJ1.CtTWP CxKCt0apTWP, C1rLTCt~OP Tote; 1rOP11POt<; KCtt exKCtOapTotc;
1rPSVUCtCJL TS KCtt OaLJl.OUtP, Cx1rOCJtiiPCtL M Jhlm KCtt TOU UWJl.CtTO<; TOU
OoVAOV CJOV TOUOS, KTA.
2) The Cod. Marc. gr. app. II 163 (beginning lost): Ed. F. Pradel,
Griechische Gebete (Giessen, 1907), p. 260,18-22:
Osoc; b aiwPw<; b A.vTpwuaJl.SPoc; cK Tijc; aixJl.aA.wuiac; Toil l'Jta{36A.ou To -ycPoc;
TWP (xp()pw7rWP, pUCJCtL TOP OOUAOP CJOV TOUTOP 1rCtC111<; CPsp-ysiac; 1rPSVJ1.CtTWP
c'xKaOapTwP, c1riTa~op Tote; 1rOP11Potc; Kat ixKaOapTotc; oaiJl.oUtP, c'x1rouriiPm
Cx1r0 lflc; 1/tvxf/c; KCtt TOU UWJl.CtTO<; TOU oovA.ov CJOU, KTA.
e
3) Ms. de la Lavra du Mont Athos, 20: Ed. L. Delatte, Un Office
byzantin d'exorcisme (Bruxelles, 1957), p. 39, 1f.:
·a Osoc; b aiwPwc;, b XvTpwuaJl.sPoc; To -ycPoc; TwP ixp()pw1rwP cK Tflc;
aixJl.aA.wuiac; Toil ota{36A.ov, puum Kat TOP ooi!MP uov Toi!ToP CK 7retCJ11<;

4) An exorcism (Ms. 825 Bibliotheque Nationale) attributed to Ephrem
the Syrian: A. Delatte, Anecdota Atheniensia (1927), p. 254, 14f. (giving
an entirely different beginning and omissions, e.g., of if;vxij<;):
... CtVTO<; b CxKCtTCtA111rTO<; esoc; b CxPCtACt{3WP CtVTOP TOP 'HA.ietP CP 1rVPLP£¥
CXPJl.CtTt sic; TOP ovpetPOP, CxVTO<; C1rtTtJ1.& VjJ.tP TOt<; c'xwOCtpTotc; 1rPSVJ1.CtC1tP
Cx1rOCJrfJPCtL Cx1r0 TOP OoUAOP TOU esou OOStPCt.
Our text's a1roarijvcn a1ro (rij<;) if;vxij<;, with the reminiscence of
1rvevp.cxai perhaps to be identified in the tablet's 1rav 1rvevp.cx, suggests
that our amulet preserves an ancient Vorlage of the exorcisms cited above.
The early amulet at least demonstrates how the later Greek liturgical
exorcisms attested in the Euchologia and hindred documents can trace
their ancestry to older texts of probable Hellenistic Jewish origin.

## 第412页

68 Fragment of a Christian Liturgical Exorcism
Fig. 65. Plate XXVIII
Cyprus (exact location unknown) IV A.D.
Paris, Bibliotheque Nationale H. 1.6 em.; W. 2.7 em.
Froehner No. 1212 Silver lamella
Lit. Marie-Christine Hellmann, "W. Froehner et Chypre," Cahiers du Centre d'
Etudes Chypriotes 16 (1991-92), pp. 17-28, p. 23f.
The silver lamella was purchased in Cyprus by Froehner in 1874 but
is of inexact provenance. The editio princeps of Hellmann 1991-92:23 is
based on the earlier reading of Froehner, obtained from his diaries. But
the fragile text has suffered some additional damage since Froehner's
deciphering made 120 years ago. Underlined letters indicate a portion of
the text now lost. Hellmann 1991-1992:23 gives the following description:
a
"Froehner, No VIII, 30, tr. Chypre, achat 1874: petitefeuille d'argent tres
fine et cassee de tous cotes, l. cons. 2, 7, h. cons. 1,6 em. L 'inscription
gravee invoque 'Jesus-Christ de Nazareth' (lecture Froehner verijiee par M.
C. H., restitutions D. Feissel; inedite)."
This very fragmentary piece of silver foil probably contained a
Christian liturgical exorcism, not unlike 35 in this corpus. We have
restored the text accordingly and provided supporting parallels, with the
proviso that other interpretations may be possible.
[opKirw ae OVOJLa-]
[n roil] Narwpaiov,
[r]ou 'hwov Xpwrov,
4 (K(at) TW)V Ci"yLWIJ cX11'
(0<1TOA)WV avrov,
[K(ai) rwv av')']el\)..wv
[avrov, e~eMe'iv, KTA.].
[I adjure you in the name of the] Nazoraean, Jesus Christ, and his holy
apostles and [his] angels [to come out of (so-and-so) ... ].

## 第413页

388 68 A Christian Exorcism
1 [opKitw (JB ovop.cxln Tov]: Our proposed restoration fits the lacuna
and the sense of the spell. In nearly all such liturgical creeds the invoca
tion of ecclesiastical figures, saints, and names is preceded by an adjura
tion (e~)opKitw. See Commentary, to follow.
2 [n Tov] Ncxtwpcxiou [T]ov 'hwov Xpturov: Ncxtwpcxiou I []ou Tquov
XpwTov ed. pr. The formulaic language here seems to derive from keryg
matic healing episodes found in the Book of Acts, and elsewhere: Acts
3:6: ei-Tr-ev oe IIiTpo~· CtP'YVPWV KCXL xpuuiov oux inn:Xpxet p.Ot, 0 oe exw
TOVTO (JOt oiowp.t" ev TW OVOP,CXTL 'Inuov XpWTOV TOV Ncxtwpcxiou
1r8pt 'TraTet; Acts 4: 10: 'YVW(JTOV BUTW 'TrOtUtV vp.7v KCXL 1rCXVTL T(iJ P.cx(iJ
w
'lupcx~A OTt ev OVOU.CXTL 'lnuov XOLUTOV TOU Ncxtwpcxiou, ov vp.e'i~
BUTCXUpWUCXTB, ov 0 Oeo~ ij'Yetpev BK V8Kpwv, ev TOUT'-IJ O~TO~ 1rCXpBUT'YJK8V
BVW'TrWV vp.wv V'Yt~~; Epiphanius, Haer. 25. 345,20: ev ovop.CXTL 'lnuov TOV
Ncxtwpcxiou TOV umupwOivTo~' efeAOe et'Tr' CXUTOU TO OCXLJ.l.OVWV KCXL V'YL~~
'YBV'YJTCXt. But the order of the liturgical formula here is different, as the
others have Ncxtwpcxiou following, not precede, 'lrJuov XpwTov. On the
problem surrounding NcxtcxprJVO~ versus Ncxtwpcx7o~, see Bauer-Arndt
Gingrich-Danker, A Greek-English Lexicon (Chicago, 1979), s. v.
3-4 [- - Tw]v Cx'YLWV a7r[OUTOA]wv cxuTov: The photograph suggests
rather ... r.wv a1roUToP.wv, but this is due to the horizontal of the gamma
extending over the iota. This phrase is very common among Patristic
writers, but I have not found it connected with "the Nazoraean, Jesus
Christ" - nor with angels - except in the context of liturgical
exorcisms; cf. A. Delatte, Anecdota Atheniensia I (Paris & Liege, 1927),
p. 62, 12f: opKitw (J8 ei~ TOt evvicx TCt'YJ.lCXTCX TWV Cx'YLWV Ct'Y'YBAWV KCXL ek
TOV~ t{3' Ct'TrOUTOAOU~, KTA.; idem, p. 119,25: opKitw up.&~ KCXTOt TWV t{3'
Ct'TrOUTOAWV" e~e'AOcxTe KCXL avcxxwp~UCXTB, KTA.; see, further, idem, p. 83,
16. The apostles are invoked both confessionally and for the exorcistic
healing powers that they make claim to through the name of Jesus Christ.
5 [K(cx£) Twv Ct'Y'Y]eA.'Awv: Only the barest trace of this former read
ing is extant. A gold phylactery (to be re-edited in a subsequent volume of
Greek Magical Amulets) similarly names the Lord and his angels: see the
editio princeps, A. Greifenhagen, Schmuckarbeiten in Edelmetall, II:
Einzelstucke (Berlin, 1975), p. 99, Nr. 12: e'Yw 'YOtP eip.(t)
